Gallegos v. State


No. 44782


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


123 Nev. 289; 163 P.3d 456; 2007 Nev. LEXIS 41; 123 Nev. Adv. Rep. 31



August 2, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count of unlawful possession of a firearm. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Nancy M. Saitta, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, convicted defendant of one count of unlawful possession of a firearm in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 202.360(1)(b). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 202.360(1)(b) was unconstitutionally vague as it failed to define the term "fugitive from justice." The supreme court concluded that defendant met his burden by clearly showing that § 202.360(1)(b) was unconstitutionally vague because it failed to give notice of what conduct it prohibited and lacked the specific standards needed to avoid arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement. The Legislature did not define the term "fugitive from justice." Congress defined the term "fugitive from justice" in 18 U.S.C.S. § 921(a)(15). The Legislature failed to provide the public with statutory notice what the term meant. Nev. Rev. Stat. § 202.360(1)(b) caused ordinary citizens to have to guess at its meaning and failed to give notice of the law so they could conform their conduct to its requirements. The statute was susceptible to arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement because it did not specify the circumstances under which a person could be arrested and prosecuted as a fugitive from justice in possession of a firearm. It established no clear standards to guide law enforcement officials, and, ultimately, jurors as to whether a violation had occurred.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

482 of 673 DOCUMENTS



Halverson v. Hardcastle


No. 49453


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


123 Nev. 245; 163 P.3d 428; 2007 Nev. LEXIS 38; 123 Nev. Adv. Rep. 29



July 27, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at In re Halverson, 169 P.3d 1161, 2007 Nev. LEXIS 57 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Original petition for a writ of quo warranto challenging a chief district judge's authority to require another district judge to meet with a committee of judges, to remove and reassign that judge's criminal caseload, and to temporarily bar that judge from entering the courthouse.



DISPOSITION: Petition granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner district judge sought a writ of quo warranto under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 35.010(1) challenging respondent chief judge's authority to appoint a three-judge committee to work with the district judge in improving her judicial performance, reassigning the district judge's criminal caseload to another judge, and barring the district judge from the courthouse until she agreed to meet with the committee to address security concerns.


OVERVIEW: After receiving information that the district judge was behavior inappropriately with jurors and staff members, the chief judge appointed a three-judge committee to review the complaints. After the district judge failed to cooperate with the committee and obtained her own bodyguards to accompany her to the courthouse, the chief judge reassigned her criminal caseload and barred her from the courthouse. On the district judge's petition for a writ of quo warranto, the court held that while the chief judge properly appointed the three-judge committee under Nev. 8th J. Dist. Ct. R. 1.30(b)(16) to address issues related to the district judge's conduct on and off the bench, the chief judge did not have the authority to require the district judge to meet with the committee or to ban her for failing to do so. The proper remedy, absent an emergency situation, would be either with the court or the judicial discipline commission. Because the chief judge had valid reasons of convenience or necessity for reassigning the district judge's criminal cases to another judge, quo warranto did not lie to prevent the chief judge from doing so.


OUTCOME: The district judge's petition was granted as to the chief judge's order barring the district judge from the courthouse. The petition was otherwise denied.


 

483 of 673 DOCUMENTS



Arata v. Faubion


No. 43993


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


123 Nev. 153; 161 P.3d 244; 2007 Nev. LEXIS 29; 123 Nev. Adv. Rep. 19



June 28, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Arata v. Faubion, 2007 Nev. LEXIS 65 (Nev., Nov. 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from a judgment entered upon a jury verdict in a personal injury action and from an order denying a motion for a new trial. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; David Wall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a driver, his mother, and his stepfather, challenged a decision from the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which entered judgment in favor of respondent pedestrian in a negligence action.


OVERVIEW: The driver struck the pedestrian in a crosswalk while operating a vehicle owned by his mother and stepfather. The driver was 19, but he lived with his mother and stepfather. Thereafter, the pedestrian filed an action against the driver, the mother, and the stepfather under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 41.440. Judgment was entered for the pedestrian, and this appeal followed. In partially affirming, the supreme court determined that a challenge to § 41.440 under U.S. Const. amend. XIV based on due process and equal protection was meritless. A heightened level of scrutiny did not apply because no fundamental rights were implicated; moreover, there was no discrimination against a suspect class. Even though there was no legislative history to examine, the supreme court found that there were at least two rational bases for the enactment of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 41.440. As such, the statute was constitutional. Next, liability was properly imposed upon the mother because of the family relationship. There was no distinction in the statute between dependent and adult children. However, it was error to find that the stepfather was an immediate family member as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The portion of the decision finding that the stepfather was an immediate family member of the driver was reversed, and the case was remanded for a new trial on that issue. The rest of the decision was affirmed.


 

484 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Thomas v. State


No. 46509


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 1361; 148 P.3d 727; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 147; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 114



December 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Thomas v. Nev., 128 S. Ct. 1061, 169 L. Ed. 2d 806, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 874 (U.S., Jan. 14, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a sentence of death following a second penalty hearing after remand. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Sally L. Loehrer, Judge.
Thomas v. State, 120 Nev. 37, 83 P.3d 818, 2004 Nev. LEXIS 7 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), found defendant guilty of, inter alia, two counts of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon. After a penalty hearing, the jury returned two verdicts of death for the murders. However, the supreme court remanded the case for a new penalty hearing. After new deliberations, the jury again returned two verdicts of death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant gave a statement in allocution, in which he expressed remorse and asked for forgiveness for stealing two precious lives. The jury found seven mitigating factors, in that he had: (1) accepted responsibility for the crimes; (2) cooperated with the investigation; (3) demonstrated remorse; (4) counseled others against criminal acts; (5) suffered learning and emotional disabilities; (6) found religion; and (7) been denied by his father. But, the jurors determined that the aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating factors and returned two verdicts of death. Although defendant's childhood and upbringing were certainly not the best, and he apparently had made some effort to counsel others against taking his path, the facts of the case were compelling: (1) he robbed his former employer at gunpoint; (2) left the actual robbery to seek out two potential witnesses, the victims; and (3) stabbed the victims to death. The victims were young men who should have been safe at their place of work. Also, defendant had a violent criminal history and had shown a capacity for continued violence while in prison. Thus, the supreme court concluded that the death sentences were not excessive.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Santana v. State


No. 44852


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 1458; 148 P.3d 741; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 140; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 121



December 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability granted, in part, Related proceeding at Santana v. Neven, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 92721 (D. Nev., Aug. 18, 2011)
Related proceeding at Santana v. State, 2014 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 1311 (2014)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Santana v. State, 2014 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 1572 (2014)
Related proceeding at Santana v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court of Nev., 2015 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 1088 (2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, upon jury verdict, of 19 counts of coercion. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Valorie Vega, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which convicted him of 19 counts of felony coercion, in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 207.190(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was incarcerated during the summer of 2002. While he was there, he placed random telephone calls to several women and children in southern California. Each call began with a recording, which explained that the call was a collect call from the prison. The recipient of the call was then invited to accept or decline the call. Each victim accepted. Once defendant heard the victim accept the call, he informed her that the recording was a joke and then initiated a friendly conversation. Soon after, defendant told the victim that he was near her location, with a weapon, and that he would harm her if she did not participate in a sexual conversation with him. Defendant was subsequently convicted of 19 counts of felony coercion. He received a life sentence on each of the 19 counts. On appeal, the court found that the trial court erred in not instructing the jury to apply the reasonable person test. Therefore, the jury instructions were erroneous. Because the court did not know what the jury would have found in terms of the reasonable person test, it was not clear beyond a reasonable doubt that the jury would have found defendant guilty of felony coercion absent the error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions and remanded the case for a new trial.

State ex rel. State Bd. of Equalization v. Bakst


No. 46752


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 1403; 148 P.3d 717; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 143; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 116



December 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State ex rel. State Bd. of Equalization v. Barta, 124 Nev. 612, 188 P.3d 1092, 2008 Nev. LEXIS 63 (2008)
Related proceeding at Vill. League v. State, 125 Nev. 1086, 281 P.3d 1227, 2009 Nev. LEXIS 2141 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order granting petition for judicial review and complaint for relief under NRS 361.420 and overturning a decision of the Nevada State Board of Equalization regarding real property located in Washoe County. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; William A. Maddox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and county, sought review of the judgment of the First Judicial District Court, Carson City (Nevada) that overturned a decision of the Nevada State Board of Equalization regarding assessments rendered against real property owned by respondents, taxpayers, located in Washoe County.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers owned real property near Lake Tahoe. After receiving dramatically increased tax bills, the taxpayers questioned the methods utilized by the assessor to value their real property. The taxpayers filed individual petitions for review with the county board of equalization in which they asserted that the assessor used unauthorized methodologies to value the land. The county upheld the assessments and the taxpayers appealed to the Nevada State Board of Equalization (State Board). The State Board held that the assessor's methodologies for appraising the properties was appropriate. The supreme court found that the methodologies used were invalid. Specifically, their inconsistent application violated the uniform and equal rate of assessment required by Nev. Const. art. 10. The 2003-2004 valuations, which were based on those methodologies, were therefore unjust and inequitable. Any taxes collected that could be attributed to those invalid methodologies were unconstitutional, as determined by the district court, and the taxpayers who paid such taxes were entitled to a refund.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Calvin v. State


No. 46641


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 1178; 147 P.3d 1097; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 131; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 100



December 14, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Calvin v. State, 2007 Nev. LEXIS 2 (Nev., Jan. 31, 2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Claim dismissed by Calvin v. McDaniels, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 56724 (D. Nev., June 30, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a guilty plea, of two counts of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Jackie Glass, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) to two counts of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon. After defendant's motion to withdraw his guilty plea was denied, defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 178.400 failed to comply with federal constitutional standards for competency to stand trial, as set out in Dusky, and that his competency proceedings were therefore invalid. The supreme court disagreed. Section 178.400 conformed to that of Dusky and thus satisfied constitutional requirements. Thus, consistent with Dusky, under Nevada statutory law, a defendant was incompetent to stand trial if he either was not of sufficient mentality to be able to understand the nature of the criminal charges against him or he was not able to aid and assist his counsel in the defense interposed upon the trial or against the pronouncement of the judgment thereafter. In addition, while a witness's testimony might have been relevant, the district court refused to allow her to testify until she turned over her notes of her meetings with defendant to the State. Defendant did not demonstrate that that condition was improper. Further, defendant failed to show how either of his proposed witness's testimony would have led the district court to conclude that he was not competent to stand trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

488 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Bejarano v. State


No. 44297


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 1066; 146 P.3d 265; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 122; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 92



November 16, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus in a death penalty case. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Janet J. Berry, Judge.
Bejarano v. State, 104 Nev. 851, 809 P.2d 598, 1988 Nev. LEXIS 480 (1988)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada), denied appellant inmate's post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus in a death penalty case where the inmate was found guilty of murdering and robbing the victim, a taxicab driver. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: Although predicated upon existing legal principles, McConnell v. State set forth a new rule of death penalty law in Nevada. Under the McConnell case, it was impermissible under the United States and Nevada Constitutions to base an aggravating circumstance in a capital prosecution on the felony upon which a felony murder was predicated. Absent retroactive application of the rule in the McConnell case, there would be a significant risk that a defendant faced a punishment that the law could not impose. Thus, the supreme court concluded that the McConnell case announced a substantive rule of law that had to be applied retroactively. Having concluded that the McConnell case set forth a new substantive rule that demanded retroactive application, the supreme court applied it to the inmate's case. Thus, the robbery and receiving-money aggravating factors were removed from consideration. However, four valid aggravating factors supporting the inmate's death sentence remained. It was clear beyond a reasonable doubt that absent the invalid aggravating factors, the jury would have still sentenced the inmate to death. Therefore, the inmate was not entitled to any post-conviction relief.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the district court's judgment denying the inmate's petition for post-conviction relief.

City of Las Vegas v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 45222


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 1041; 146 P.3d 240; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 119; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 90



November 9, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original petition for a writ of certiorari challenging the district court's affirmance of the Las Vegas Municipal Court's determinations that portions of the Las Vegas Municipal Code involving erotic dancing are unconstitutionally vague and overbroad.



DISPOSITION: Petition granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city filed a petition for a writ of certiorari against respondents, the Eighth Judicial District Court (Nevada) and others, seeking a determination that the municipal court lacked jurisdiction to consider the constitutionality of Las Vegas, Nevada, Municipal Code 6.35.100(I) and that the district court improperly concluded that the municipal code provision was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad.


OVERVIEW: Municipal courts had jurisdiction to consider the constitutionality of misdemeanor ordinances that a city sought to enforce as they had original jurisdiction over ordinance-based misdemeanors committed in their respective cities pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 5.050(2). It was illogical to conclude that only district courts had jurisdiction to determine the constitutionality of misdemeanor ordinances when municipal courts had original jurisdiction to try cases under misdemeanor ordinances in the first instance. Las Vegas, Nevada, Municipal Code 6.35.100(I) was not unconstitutionally vague because when viewed in the context of other ordinances regulating erotic dance establishments, it required an intent to sexually arouse or excite. That construction adequately put dancers and patrons on notice as to what conduct was prohibited, permitted touching that was not intended to sexually arouse or excite, and provided an adequate standard for law enforcement. The ordinance was not unconstitutionally overbroad because even if fondling and caressing were protected expressive conduct when part of an erotic dance, the ordinance was a legitimate time, place, and manner restriction.


OUTCOME: The city's petition was granted with respect to the constitutional issues. The court clerk was directed to issue a writ of certiorari instructing the district court to reverse that portion of its order affirming the municipal court's determination that the ordinance was unconstitutional.

Sheriff v. Witzenburg


No. 45462


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 1056; 145 P.3d 1002; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 121; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 91



November 9, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order granting a pretrial petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Lee A. Gates, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with various property crimes against three alleged out-of-state victims. After the matter was bound over, defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus with the district court. The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, granted the writ petition and dismissed the charges that related to the out-of-state witnesses' affidavits. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's preliminary examination, the State introduced the alleged victims' affidavits in lieu of their personal appearance, as permitted under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 171.197. Defendant contended that this practice violated defendant's Sixth Amendment Confrontation Clause right. At issue on appeal was whether the Confrontation Clause and jurisprudence from the United States Supreme Court applied at a preliminary examination. The supreme court concluded that the Sixth Amendment Confrontation Clause and Crawford decision did not apply to a preliminary examination because the confrontation right was historically described as a trial right. The supreme court further concluded that the statutory right to cross-examination, under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 171.196(5), was a qualified right, subject to the exception under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 171.197, which allowed the State to use an affidavit when a witness either resided outside of Nevada or more than 100 miles from the preliminary examination's location.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the district court's order granting defendant's pretrial habeas corpus petition.

State v. Colosimo


No. 45528


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 950; 142 P.3d 352; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 105; 33 A.L.R.6th 785; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 81



September 14, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order granting a motion to dismiss an indictment charging respondent with the use of technology to lure children, a violation of NRS 201.560. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Brent T. Adams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged a decision from the Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada), which dismissed an indictment against defendant under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.560.


OVERVIEW: Defendant corresponded through the Internet with an undercover police officer. When he arrived at a prearranged place with the alleged intent of having sex with a minor, he was arrested and charged with using technology to lure children. The indictment was later dismissed, and the State sought review. In affirming, the supreme court determined that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.560 was not vague, in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment. The proscribed conduct was clearly defined and persons of ordinary intelligence had fair notice of what conduct was permitted. Further, it was not overbroad since the inducement of minors to engage in illegal sexual activity enjoyed no First Amendment protection. Also, § 201.560 did not violate the Commerce Clause because, even though the electronic messages might have briefly been transmitted to places outside of Nevada, criminalizing such transgressions could have hardly been considered a burden on interstate commerce. The evidence did not support defendant's contention that he was entrapped. However, because the actual intended victim was not less than 16 years of age, the prosecution could not have established that element of the crime.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

Herbst Gaming, Inc. v. Heller


No. 47620


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 877; 141 P.3d 1224; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 103; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 78



September 8, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying injunctive and declaratory relief with respect to the Nevada Clean Indoor Air Act initiative's placement on the November 2006 ballot. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; William A. Maddox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part with instructions to Secretary of State and vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant opponents brought an action for injunctive and declaratory relief, seeking to remove the Nevada Clean Indoor Air Act initiative from the general election ballot. The First Judicial District Court, Carson City, Nevada, denied relief. The opponents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The opponents were business entities, such as bars, taverns, and convenience and grocery stores with gaming areas, where smoking was permitted, and their related organizations. If the initiative was passed, these entities would be compelled to further restrict smoking and in some instances prohibit it entirely. The instant court concluded that preelection challenges to the measure's substantive constitutionality were not ripe for judicial review. The instant court also concluded that the initiative's title clearly stated that the measure's purpose was to prohibit smoking in most public places, with the exception of casinos and "stand-alone bars." However, in order to clarify the initiative's coverage with respect to bars, the instant court directed respondent Nevada Secretary of State to clearly indicate that the measure covered all bars with food-handling licenses. The initiative did not violate Nev. Const. art. XIX, § 6's appropriation or expenditure provision because the expense inherent in the general enforcement of criminal statutes did not constitute an expenditure. Thus, the initiative's lack of a funding mechanism did not render it invalid.


OUTCOME: The district court's order denying injunctive and declaratory relief was affirmed. However, the district court lacked authority to issue an advisory opinion concerning whether the measure might be interpreted to include hotel and motel rooms; accordingly, its ruling with respect to this issue was vacated.

Nevadans for the Prot. of Prop. Rights, Inc. v. Heller


No. 47825


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 894; 141 P.3d 1235; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 104; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 79



September 8, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected.
Rehearing denied by Nevadans for Protection of Property Rights v. Heller, 2006 Nev. LEXIS 107 (Nev., Sept. 18, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying declaratory and/or writ relief to prevent placement of the Nevada Property Owners' Bill of Rights on the November 2006 general election ballot. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Mark R. Denton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individuals and government entities (entities) opposed respondent property owners' Nevada Property Owners' Bill of Rights initiative, and filed a complaint seeking declaratory and extraordinary relief to prevent the initiative from being placed on the ballot. The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) denied all requested relief finding the initiative complied with Nev. Rev. Stat. § 295.009. The entities appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court found that the initiative encompassed only a single subject and was therefore not disqualified from appearing on the ballot. Section 295.009 placed a single-subject requirement on initiative petitions. The issue for the Supreme Court of Nevada was whether the initiative petition violated that requirement. The supreme court concluded that § 295.009 was constitutional and that because the Nevada Property Owners' Bill of Rights embraced more than one subject, the initiative violated the statute. Even so, strong public policy favored upholding the initiative power whenever possible, and § 295.009 did not prescribe a remedy for single-subject requirement violations. Because the initiative included a severability clause and facially and unequivocally pertained to a primary subject, eminent domain, the supreme court was compelled to sever sections 1 and 8, which did not pertain to eminent domain, in lieu of removing the entire initiative from the ballot. Further, the supreme court confirmed that initiatives proposing constitutional amendments had to propose policy and not direct administrative details.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part. The clerk of the supreme court was directed to issue the remittitur forthwith.

 

494 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Nevadans for Nev. v. Beers


No. 47814


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 930; 142 P.3d 339; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 102; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 80



September 8, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying appellants' complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief, seeking to prohibit the Tax and Spending Control for Nevada Initiative from appearing on the November 2006 general election ballot. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; William A. Maddox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant opponents brought a complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief, seeking to prohibit a tax and spending control ballot initiative submitted by respondent committee from appearing on the general election ballot. The First Judicial District Court, Carson City, Nevada, denied the complaint for relief and refused to prohibit respondent Nevada Secretary of State from placing the initiative on the ballot. The opponents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The initiative petition that was circulated for signatures differed from the petition that the committee had filed with the Secretary of State. The instant court concluded that the district court erred in denying the opponents request for declaratory and injunctive relief because the initiative petition did not strictly adhere to the requirements of Nev. Const. art. XIX, § 2(4). The record demonstrated that the difference between the filed version and the circulated version was material and substantial. The circulated version allowed a 21 percent increase in state spending during the initial budget cycle, while the filed version allowed only a 7.4 percent increase in spending. The primary purpose of the measure would not be effectuated under the circulated version. The circulated petition was not an accurate reflection of the committee's intent to implement a constitutional limit on government spending and was therefore misleading. Because the committee did not strictly adhere to the requirements of Nev. Const. art. XIX, § 2(4) by filing a true copy of the initiative petition with the Secretary of State, the initiative necessarily failed.


OUTCOME: The district court's order was reversed. The Secretary of State was prohibited from placing the initiative on the ballot.

 

495 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Pantano v. State


No. 43426


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 782; 138 P.3d 477; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 90; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 68



July 20, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Pantano v. Nevada, 549 U.S. 1126, 127 S. Ct. 957, 166 L. Ed. 2d 728, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 252 (2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Pantano v. Donat, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 28387 (D. Nev., Mar. 7, 2011)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied, Dismissed by Pantano v. Donat, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 127376 (D. Nev., Sept. 5, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, upon jury verdict, of one count of sexual assault of a child under 14. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Valorie Vega, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) of one count of sexual assault of a child under 14. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 51.385 was unconstitutional as applied to him. The supreme court disagreed. Defendant had an adequate opportunity to cross-examine the child. The fact that she answered negatively or "I don't know" when asked on cross-examination to identify to whom she spoke regarding the incident did not render the cross-examination ineffective. If anything, such testimony served to undermine her testimony and that of the surrogate witnesses. The child's availability for cross-examination at trial defeated defendant's "as-applied" challenge to § 51.385. In addition, the child's statements to her father were nontestimonial in nature. A parent questioning his or her child regarding possible sexual abuse was inquiring into the health, safety, and well-being of the child. To characterize such parental questioning as the gathering of evidence for purposes of litigation would unnecessarily and undesirably militate against a parent's ability to support and nurture a child at a time when the child most needed that support.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Ford v. State


No. 43473


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 796; 138 P.3d 500; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 91; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 69



July 20, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Ford v. State, 2006 Nev. LEXIS 111 (Nev., Sept. 27, 2006)
Related proceeding at Ford v. State, 125 Nev. 1036, 281 P.3d 1172, 2009 Nev. LEXIS 316 (2009)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Ford v. State, 2012 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 1693 (2012)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion granted by Ford v. Williams, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 47580 (D. Nev., Apr. 2, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count of second-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and one count of burglary while in possession of a deadly weapon. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph T. Bonaventure, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which convicted him of one count of second-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and one count of burglary while in possession of a deadly weapon.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of one count of second-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and one count of burglary while in possession of a deadly weapon. Defendant was 15 years old at the time of the offenses. On appeal, the court found that because defendant was properly detained and consented to being interviewed, his argument that the traffic citation statute, Nev. Rev. Stat. § 62C.070, precluded his detention was without merit. An officer had reasonable grounds to suspect that he was the individual described in a police report and therefore had lawful grounds to detain him. Because his right to parental notification did not bear on the authority of the investigators to interview him, Nev. Rev. Stat. § 62C.010 did not operate as a procedural bar to the admissibility of an otherwise voluntary statement. Further, because defendant was lawfully detained, and the officers' observation of blood on his stocking cap and sweatshirt was inadvertent and immediately apparent to be evidence of a crime, the admission of the stocking cap and sweatshirt did not violate his rights under the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.

 

497 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Ennis v. State


No. 43556


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 694; 137 P.3d 1095; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 78; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 60



July 13, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Ennis v. Neven, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 18856 (D. Nev., Mar. 14, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court denying a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Nancy M. Saitta, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed a judgment of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), denying his postconviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus, which challenged his convictions of second-degree murder with use of a deadly weapon, coercion with physical force, and attempted murder.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted for stabbing the victim, his girlfriend, to death. At trial, the inmate admitting to stabbing his girlfriend but claimed that it was in self-defense. On appeal, the court held that because the inmate's conviction became final before the Crawford case was decided and because the Crawford decision did not apply retroactively, the inmate was not entitled to relief on his claim that statements by officers and family members constituted testimonial hearsay that would have been excluded under Crawford. The inmate was not entitled to relief on his claim that his counsel was ineffective for failing to object to the testimony of the victim's aunt, who was talking to the victim's grandmother at the time of the attack and placed the inmate at the murder scene, because the inmate failed to show that he was prejudiced, as he admitted to being at the house and killing the victim. In addition, counsel did object to the testimony, a better foundation for its was laid, and the aunt's testimony clearly fell within the excited utterance exception to the hearsay rule under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 51.095.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying the inmate's petition for a writ of habeas corpus was affirmed.

McCarran Int'l Airport v. Sisolak


No. 41646


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 645; 137 P.3d 1110; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 80; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 58



July 13, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McCarran Int'l Airport v. Sisolak, 549 U.S. 1206, 127 S. Ct. 1260, 167 L. Ed. 2d 76, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 2086 (2007)
Related proceeding at N. Am. Props. v. McCarran Int'l Airport, 2016 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 487 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an inverse condemnation judgment. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Mark R. Denton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an inverse condemnation case, the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, concluded that the height restriction ordinances imposed by appellant, Clark County, Nevada, effectuated a per se taking. A jury awarded respondent landowner compensatory damages of $ 6.5 million, attorney fees, costs and prejudgment interest. The County appealed.


OVERVIEW: Clark County operated the primary commercial airport serving southern Nevada. The County adopted height restriction ordinances limiting the development of a landowner's airspace. As a resident of Nevada, the landowner held a property right in the useable airspace above his property up to 500 feet. The district court found that airplanes were flying through airspace at an altitude lower than 500 feet. Because the height restriction ordinances authorized airplanes to make a permanent, physical invasion of the landowner's airspace, a Loretto-type regulatory per se taking occurred, requiring an award of just compensation. The district court did not abuse its discretion by awarding prejudgment interest.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

499 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Silvar v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 44825


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 289; 129 P.3d 682; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 29; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 25



March 16, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original petition for a writ of certiorari challenging a district court decision upholding the constitutionality of Clark County Ordinance 12.08.030.



DISPOSITION: Petition granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner arrestee was accused of allegedly violating Clark County, Nevada, Ordinance (CCO) 12.08.030. The justice court dismissed the complaint, concluding that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. Respondent district court reversed and remanded, upholding the ordinance's constitutionality. The arrestee filed a petition for a writ of certiorari, in an original proceeding, challenging the district court's decision.


OVERVIEW: The arrestee allegedly asked an undercover officer if he was "dating," a street term synonymous with seeking prostitution. At issue before the supreme court was whether the county's prostitution loitering ordinance was unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. The supreme court agreed that the ordinance was vague and overly broad. Because CCO 12.08.030 lacked any guiding circumstances, the ordinance had an even broader sweep than similar stricken ordinances, thus heightening its unconstitutionality. Further, the conduct the ordinance criminalized was constitutionally protected. Because CCO 12.08.030 chilled this conduct, the ordinance was substantially overbroad in relation to its legitimate sweep. Also, the ordinance's provision for an opportunity to explain one's conduct did not mitigate this chilling effect. Second, the ordinance contained no specific intent element; therefore, it could not make constitutionally protected conduct criminal. The supreme court concluded that the county's prostitution loitering ordinance, 12.08.030, was both unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. Therefore, the ordinance was void.


OUTCOME: The supreme court granted the petition; it directed the clerk to issue a writ of certiorari instructing the district court to vacate its order, which reversed and remanded the justice court's order.

Sustainable Growth Initiative Comm. v. Jumpers, LLC


No. 41118


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 53; 128 P.3d 452; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 9; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 7



February 9, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal and cross-appeals from a district court summary judgment order in a ballot initiative matter. Ninth Judicial District Court, Douglas County; Michael P. Gibbons, Judge.
Garvin v. Ninth Judicial Dist. Court, 118 Nev. 749, 59 P.3d 1180, 2002 Nev. LEXIS 90 (2002).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant political action committee challenged a decision of the Ninth Judicial District Court, Douglas County, Nevada, which found that a sustainable growth initiative (SGI) conflicted with the county's master plan and was void ab initio in an action filed by respondents, the county, businesses, and individuals seeking injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The SGI limited building to 280 units per annum. The court held that to have legal effect, the SGI must be enacted as a zoning ordinance and thus was subject to substantial compliance with the master plan under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 278.250. The court also held that because the SGI clearly mirrored the policies and concerns of the master plan, it was substantially compliant with the master plan policies and goals. The master plan suggested a growth rate between 2 and 3.5 % annually, and the building cap in the SGI reflected approximately a 2 % increase, which was consistent with the plan. The fact that the county secured its bonds based on a 3.5 % annual growth rate, without any indication that the actual growth rate that would be implemented in the county would be 3.5 % did not render the SGI inconsistent. Further, the SGI did not preclude the county from implementing its incentive program to encourage developers to provide affordable housing, and by its plain language, Nev. Rev. Stat. § 278.0201(3) did not prohibit the SGI. Because the building cap was based on a 2 % growth rate, the 280-unit cap was not arbitrary and capricious.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's orders.

Int'l Game Tech., Inc. v. Second Judicial Dist. Court


No. 43882, No. 43953


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


122 Nev. 132; 127 P.3d 1088; 2006 Nev. LEXIS 16; 122 Nev. Adv. Rep. 13



February 9, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Int'l Game Tech., Inc. v. Second Judicial Dist. Court, 179 P.3d 556, 2008 Nev. LEXIS 18 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Consolidated original petitions for writs of mandamus or prohibition challenging district court orders denying motions to dismiss false claims actions.



DISPOSITION: Petitions granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In complex litigation, petitioners, corporations, partnerships, and the Nevada Attorney General, brought petitions for extraordinary relief to challenge the decisions of respondents, district courts and district court judges, refusing to dismiss actions brought under Nevada's False Claims Act (FCA). The real parties in interest retailers joined the petition. The matters were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, who were private citizens in the underlying suit, brought false claims actions based on alleged tax deficiencies against the retailers. In his motion, the Attorney General argued, inter alia, that good cause existed for dismissal. Petitioners, in their requested for extraordinary relief, asserted that the district courts wrongfully applied the good cause standard by which the Attorney General could move to dismiss false claims actions. Similarly, in the second case, the Attorney General articulated a legitimate government purpose for dismissing the actions. The supreme court concluded that the Attorney General's assertion that these FCA actions implicated issues that were better left to the tax department's expertise constituted a basis for good cause dismissal; however, the supreme court did not preclude the possibility that some tax matters could be brought under the FCA. In the instant cases, though, no party demonstrated that the Attorney General acted improperly in moving to dismiss the underlying actions; therefore, the district courts manifestly abused their discretion when they refused to dismiss the underlying tax-based false claims actions for good cause.


OUTCOME: The petitions for writs of mandamus were granted; therefore, the supreme court's clerk was directed to issue the writs directing the district courts to grant the motions to dismiss the underlying false claims actions.

Williams v. State


No. 43041


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 934; 125 P.3d 627; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 106; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 90



December 29, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, upon jury verdict, of battery with use of a deadly weapon causing substantial bodily harm. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Stephen L. Huffaker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before trial, the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), denied defendant's motion to suppress his conviction for aggravated robbery, which occurred in 1985 when he was a juvenile. After a trial, a jury selected from the third venire found defendant guilty of the battery with use of a deadly weapon resulting in substantial bodily harm to the victim. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: During jury selection, the district court dismissed the first venire because it did not reflect the racial makeup of the county. As a remedy, the district court directed the jury commissioner to specifically include African Americans in the next venire. The district court dismissed the second venire because the State was concerned over the randomness of the venire's selection since three of the first 12 jurors were African Americans. Neither defendant nor the State objected to the composition of the third venire. The supreme court concluded that the district court erred when it dismissed the second venire because no evidence in the record established that the second venire was not randomly selected and because the State's objection revealed a racial motive for dismissing the venire. The district court's order for a third venire was insufficient to cure the State's racial bias present in the dismissal of the second venire; thus, a new trial was required. Also, the supreme court concluded that the district court abused its discretion in allowing the State to impeach defendant with his conviction when he was a juvenile and the admission of that evidence was not harmless error.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the district court's judgment and remanded the case to the district court for a new trial.

City of Las Vegas v. Walsh


No. 41317


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 899; 124 P.3d 203; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 104; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 85



December 15, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gehner v. Las Vegas, 547 U.S. 1071, 126 S. Ct. 1786, 164 L. Ed. 2d 519, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 3250 (U.S., Apr. 17, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for en banc reconsideration of 120 Nev. 392, 120 Nev. 392, 91 P.3d 591 (2004). Appeal from a district court order denying a petition for a writ of mandamus challenging a municipal court's decision in a criminal proceeding. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Gene T. Porter, Judge.
City of Las Vegas v. Walsh, 100 P.3d 658, 2004 Nev. LEXIS 108 (Nev., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Petition granted; opinion withdrawn; reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The instant court issued an opinion limiting the admissibility of a registered nurse's affidavit in defendant's trial for driving under the influence. The instant court concluded the affidavit was testimonial under Crawford and admission of the affidavit violated defendant's Sixth Amendment right of confrontation. The court denied appellant city's petition for rehearing. The city petitioned for en banc reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: This case involved Nev. Rev. Stat. § 50.315(4), which permitted the admission of a nurse's affidavit to prove certain facts relating to the withdrawal of blood for testing purposes. It also implicated the construction of that statute in light of the United States Supreme Court's holding in Crawford. The instant court concluded that the affidavit complied with Nev. Rev. Stat. § 50.315(4) and was admissible. The instant court further concluded that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 50.315(4) was not unconstitutional because it adequately preserved the rights of an accused under the Confrontation Clause. The affidavit was a form and encompassed standard procedures used to withdraw blood for testing. It was developed and approved by the Committee on Testing for Intoxication. It was admissible and the municipal court and district court erred in excluding it on the basis of statutory noncompliance. Although it was admissible under state statute, it was still subject to challenge under the Sixth Amendment. Admission of the affidavit, in lieu of live testimony, would violate the Confrontation Clause. Nev. Rev. Stat. § 50.315 adequately preserved the rights of an accused under the Confrontation Clause.


OUTCOME: Motion for reconsideration was granted. The judgment was reversed and the matter was remanded to the district court with instructions to grant the writ of mandamus and direct the municipal court to determine whether defendant waived any objection to the admission of the affidavit in light of this opinion.

No. 43699


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 779; 121 P.3d 567; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 93; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 77

Habeas corpus proceeding at Blake v. State, 125 Nev. 1020, 281 P.3d 1155, 2009 Nev. LEXIS 544 (2009)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay denied by, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part, Dismissed by, in part, Motion granted by Blake v. McDaniel, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 116804 (D. Nev., Nov. 2, 2010)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Blake v. McDaniel, 2014 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 1228 (2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of two counts of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and one count of attempted murder with the use of a deadly weapon and from a sentence of death. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Sally L. Loehrer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark county (Nevada), which convicted him of first-degree murder and attempted murder. He was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot two victims to death and shot, but failed to kill, a third victim. The jury found defendant guilty of two counts of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and returned a sentence of death. The jury also found him guilty of attempted murder with the use of a deadly weapon, and he was sentenced to two consecutive terms of 96 to 240 months in prison. Defendant's rights under the Fifth, Sixth, and Fourteenth Amendments were not violated because he exercised a peremptory challenge to remove the veniremember in question.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Gaxiola v. State


No. 42258


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 633; 119 P.3d 1225; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 79; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 64



September 22, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part, Claim dismissed by Gaxiola v. Palmer, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 119668 (D. Nev., May 12, 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Certificate of appealability denied Gaxiola v. Warden, LCC, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 144419 (D. Nev., Oct. 18, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of five counts of sexual assault of a minor under the age of fourteen years and two counts of lewdness with a child under the age of fourteen years. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Michael L. Douglas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) of five counts of sexual assault of a minor under the age of fourteen years and two counts of lewdness with a child under the age of fourteen years. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to support one of the lewdness convictions because the only evidence supporting the charge was defendant's statement to the police, which he recanted at trial, and that conviction therefore, violated the corpus delicti rule. The supreme court agreed. Defendant told the police that the child would come up to him and touch defendant's penis through his boxer shorts, but defendant recanted that statement at trial, and the child did not testify that defendant made him touch his penis. Further, none of the witnesses testified that the child said that defendant made him touch defendant's penis. During the police interview, the police asked the child whether he performed that act, but the transcripts indicated that the child's responses were not audible and it was impossible to tell what those responses were. Accordingly, there was no evidence, outside of the police interview statement, that that act occurred. Thus, that conviction violated the corpus delicti rule.


OUTCOME: The convictions for sexual assault were affirmed. The lewdness convictions were reversed.

Phillips v. State


No. 42177


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 591; 119 P.3d 711; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 66; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 58



September 15, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count of aggravated stalking, twelve counts of extortion, and one count of preventing or dissuading a witness from testifying. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; John S. McGroarty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, of aggravated stalking, 12 counts of extortion in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 205.320, and one count of preventing or dissuading a witness from testifying. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sent letters and left voicemail messages claiming to be the victim's illegitimate half-brother. He stated that he was entitled to half of the money the victim inherited from their father and that if the victim did not give him money, defendant would reveal crimes committed by the victim or his father to the authorities and the media. When his demands were ignored, he threatened to kill or injure the victim. The court held that, as used in § 205.320, "libel" was the publication of a false statement of fact, "disgrace" meant to humiliate or cause loss of favor or standing, and "secret" meant a fact unfavorable one's interest and unknown to the public that one would wish to conceal. As the jury had not been instructed on these elements and returned a general verdict, it could have considered a legally insufficient theory of extortion; it might have found that defendant committed libel by making a true claim of common heritage. Other crimes evidence, which was relevant to prove that defendant was guilty of extortion, was improperly admitted because it was remote in time and prejudicial; however, the error was harmless as to the stalking and witness tampering convictions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for aggravated stalking and preventing or dissuading a witness from testifying were affirmed, but his extortion convictions were reversed and the matter was remanded for a new trial.

Weber v. State


No. 41525


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 554; 119 P.3d 107; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 71; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 57



September 15, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Weber v. Nevada, 546 U.S. 1216, 126 S. Ct. 1433, 164 L. Ed. 2d 136, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 1873 (2006)
Post-conviction proceeding at Weber v. State, 367 P.3d 833, 2010 Nev. LEXIS 2046 (Nev., 2010)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay granted by Weber v. Baker, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 147966 (D. Nev., Oct. 12, 2012)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Weber v. State, 2016 Nev. LEXIS 592 (Nev., June 24, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury trial, of sexual assault on a child under 14, use of a minor in producing pornography, possession of a visual presentation depicting sexual conduct of a person under 16, two counts of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon, two counts of attempted murder with the use of a deadly weapon, two counts of first-degree kidnapping, two counts of burglary, three counts of sexual assault on a child under 16, and three counts of open or gross lewdness, and from a sentence of death. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph T. Bonaventure, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) of sexual assault on a child under 14, use of a minor in producing pornography, possession of a visual presentation depicting sexual conduct of a person under 16, first-degree murder and attempted murder with the use of a deadly weapon, first-degree kidnapping, burglary, sexual assault on a child under 16, and open or gross lewdness. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with 17 felony counts in connection with his sexual assault of one victim and murder of other victims. He was convicted as charged and the court affirmed. While joinder of all charges in three groups was justified because the offenses in each group shared a scheme or plan common to that group, no overarching scheme or plan common to the three groups was demonstrated. Thus, the court was unable to sustain the joinder under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 173.115(2) on the ground. However, the three groups of acts were nevertheless connected together for purposes of the statute because evidence of each group would have been relevant and admissible at separate trials of the other crimes. The trial court did not err in instructing the jury on flight. Under mandatory review under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 177.055(2), the court found no error in defendant's death sentence. The evidence supported the finding of the 13 aggravating circumstances in this case, including those under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 200.033(2), (4), (8), (12). Given the appalling nature of the murder and defendant's character as revealed by his crimes, death was not an excessive sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

City Plan Dev. v. Office of the Labor Comm'r


No. 40636


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 419; 117 P.3d 182; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 58; 10 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 1495; 151 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P60,082; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 43



August 11, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying a petition for judicial review of the Labor Commissioner's decision. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Jennifer Togliatti, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Nevada Labor Commissioner determined that appellant contractor, a party to a public works contract, had failed to pay prevailing wages as required by Nev. Rev. Stat. § 338.020 and was therefore indebted to five claimants. The contractor petitioned for judicial review, which the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, denied. The contractor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner ordered the contractor to pay wages it owed the claimants, who were undocumented aliens, assessed an administrative penalty, and mandated forfeitures. The appellate court held that he acted within the scope of the authority granted by Nev. Rev. Stat. § 338.015 in conducting the hearing and that the claimants filed their wage claims within the period prescribed by Nev. Admin. Code § 607.200. That the senior investigator conducted the investigation, the chief compliance audit investigator signed the complaint, and the Commissioner acted as the hearing officer did not make the procedure unfair. Nev. Rev. Stat. § 422.065(1)(b), which denied public benefits to certain aliens, did not relieve the contractor of the duty to pay the prevailing wage to the claimants as payment of the prevailing wage was not a "public benefit" under 8 U.S.C.S. § 1621. The evidence supported the finding that the claimants were not paid prevailing wages, the wage awards, and the forfeiture. However, assessing a penalty equal to the amount of unpaid wages was improper as Nev. Rev. Stat. § 338.090(2)(a) authorized only one assessment equal to the prevailing wage-actual wage differential.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed that portion of the order with respect to the second penalty imposed and remanded to the district court with instructions to direct the Commissioner to reconsider the appropriate penalty. The order was otherwise affirmed.

 

509 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Weaver v. State


No. 41586


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 494; 117 P.3d 193; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 54; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 50



August 11, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying a petition for judicial review of an administrative decision upholding the revocation of appellant's driver's license. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Ronald D. Parraguirre, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After an accident, appellant motorist consented to a blood test, which indicated that he had a blood alcohol concentration of 0.272, an amount almost three times the legal limit in effect at the time of the accident. Thus, respondent, the Nevada Department of Motor Vehicles, revoked his driver's license. The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, upheld the revocation. The motorist appealed, asserting a due process violation.


OVERVIEW: When the officer arrived at the one vehicle accident scene, the motorist exhibited substantial signs of intoxication. However, the motorist asserted that he had walked home after the accident and had returned two hours later. At the revocation hearing, he testified that he had several beers and several shots of tequila while he was at home. The issue on appeal was whether he had a due process right at the revocation hearing to present evidence that his or alcohol level was based on alcohol consumed after driving. The Nevada Supreme Court noted that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 484.384 did not create an impermissible and irrebuttable presumption that an individual's blood alcohol content exceeded the legal limit at the time of driving. However, the supreme court held that when an individual challenged the revocation of his or her license at an administrative hearing, that hearing had to provide the opportunity for a driver to present evidence that he or she consumed alcohol only after driving. In the instant case, the motorist was given that opportunity. Unfortunately for him, the administrative law judge determined that his testimony was not credible. Thus, he was not denied due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

Garcia v. State


No. 42403


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 327; 113 P.3d 836; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 36; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 33



June 23, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Garcia v. State, 123 Nev. 813, 210 P.3d 727, 2007 Nev. LEXIS 1640 (May 31, 2007)

Writ of habeas corpus denied Garcia v. Williams, 2017 Nev. App. Unpub. LEXIS 112 (Nev. Ct. App., Feb. 23, 2017)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Garcia v. Williams, 2017 Nev. App. Unpub. LEXIS 233 (Nev. Ct. App., Apr. 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction upon a jury verdict of guilty on two counts of burglary while in possession of a firearm, two counts of robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, two counts of first-degree kidnapping with the use of a deadly weapon, one count of conspiracy to commit burglary, one count of conspiracy to commit robbery, one count of attempted robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, and three counts of false imprisonment with the use of a deadly weapon. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Michael L. Douglas, Judge.
Garcia v. State, 121 Nev. 1126, 152 P.3d 768, 2005 Nev. LEXIS 713 (Feb. 11, 2005)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) of 12 counts of robbery, attempted robbery, first-degree kidnapping, and conspiracy stemming from his participation in two of the four incidents. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that his convictions for false imprisonment in the robbery must be reversed because the district court did not properly instruct the jury on the elements of the offense. The supreme court agreed. Defendant contended that to convict him of false imprisonment, the jury had to find that the detention of the victims was not incidental to the robbery. Defendant conceded that false imprisonment was a lesser-included offense of the crime of kidnapping, but he argued that because some movement or confinement was inherent in any robbery, punishment for his robbery and false imprisonment amounted to a double punishment not contemplated by the legislature. Defendant attempted to rob three individuals and then directed them to a back room. He did not tie them up, but merely closed the door and attempted to lock them in. Therefore, the facts that were the basis of the false imprisonment convictions were part of and incidental to the conviction of attempting to rob the three individuals taken to the back room. In addition, because there was no proof of a separate agreement, defendant's conviction for conspiracy to rob one victim could not stand.


OUTCOME: The false imprisonment and conspiracy to commit robbery convictions were reversed. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

 

511 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Jordan v. State ex rel. DMV & Pub. Safety


No. 38189, No. 39052


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


121 Nev. 44; 110 P.3d 30; 2005 Nev. LEXIS 7; 121 Nev. Adv. Rep. 7



April 14, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Proper person appeals from district court orders granting summary judgment in an intentional tort action (Docket No. 38189) and dismissing an intentional tort/negligence action for failure to post security for costs (Docket No. 39052). First Judicial District Court, Carson City; Michael R. Griffin, Judge (Docket No. 38189); Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Kathy A. Hardcastle, Judge (Docket No. 39052).
In re Luckett, 232 Cal. App. 3d 107, 283 Cal. Rptr. 312, 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 790 (Cal. App. 4th Dist., 1991)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded (Docket No. 38189); affirmed and remanded with instructions (Docket No. 39052).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an arrestee and an alleged scam victim, in two separate actions against defendant State and defendant hotel owner filed proper person appeals from the First Judicial District Court, Carson City, Nevada, and Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, which dismissed plaintiffs' cases that were filed in forma pauperis and restricted their court access regarding new filings for filing meritless claims under Nev. R. Civ. P. 11.


OVERVIEW: The arrestee's action was against the State for false imprisonment among other things. The scam victim's case was against the hotel owner for not warning him about a con artist. The appeals involved first impression issues regarding orders that placed permanent restrictions on the access of litigants with in forma pauperis status to state courts. The court held that the restrictive order against the arrestee was not proper because the trial court never warned the arrestee, the order did not sufficiently indicate that he had previously instituted other meritless suits, and it failed to set a standard against which future court-access could be determined. Also, summary judgment was not proper in that case because there were genuine issues of fact, and the justice of the peace's determination to hold the arrestee was not irrebuttable evidence of probable cause to arrest. The court held that the order regarding the scam victim was different as he was warned and there were substantive findings as to the frivolous nature of his prior claims. While the trial court abused its discretion in dismissing the complaint for failure to post security, the complaint clearly failed to state a claim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing the scam victim's complaint and concluded that the vexatious litigant order restricting his access to the court was warranted. The court remanded the arrestee's case to the district court.
