McConnell v. State


No. 42101


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 1043; 102 P.3d 606; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 138; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 105



December 29, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by McConnell v. State, 121 Nev. 25, 107 P.3d 1287, 2005 Nev. LEXIS 5 (2005)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed McConnell v. State, 212 P.3d 307, 2009 Nev. LEXIS 31 (2009)
Habeas corpus proceeding at McConnell v. Baker, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 105487 (D. Nev., July 27, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] This is an appeal from a judgment of conviction of first-degree murder, pursuant to a guilty plea, and from a sentence of death, pursuant to a jury verdict. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Steven R. Kosach, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada), which convicted him of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: The State charged defendant with murder and sought the death penalty. After his preliminary examination, defendant was allowed to represent himself, and he pleaded guilty. He presented a case in mitigation at his penalty hearing, but the jury returned a sentence of death. He appealed, but the court affirmed the judgment. The court concluded that in a prosecution seeking death for a felony murder, an aggravator based on the underlying felony did not constitutionally narrow death eligibility. However, because defendant admitted to deliberate, premeditated murder, the State's alternative theory of felony murder was of no consequence and provided no ground for relief. Nevada's use of lethal injection as the method of execution was constitutional. The district court properly admitted several pieces of "bad act" evidence against defendant during the penalty hearing. There was no evidence of prosecutorial misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

513 of 673 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sutton


No. 39568


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 972; 103 P.3d 8; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 129; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 99



December 28, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a final judgment in an employment case. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Mark W. Gibbons, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state university sought review of a judgment of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which denied the university's motion for summary judgment and entered judgment for respondent former professor in his action claiming breach of contract and breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing and seeking declaratory relief for alleged violations of his substantive and procedural due process rights.


OVERVIEW: After tendering an employment contract to the professor for 1999-2000 pursuant to a 1999 court order, the university proceeded to terminate his employment as a result of his unsatisfactory evaluations in 1990 and 1991. The district court properly denied the university's motion for summary judgment and allowed the breach-of-contract claims to proceed to trial because the termination hearing violated the specific terms of the 1999-2000 contract. It was thus unnecessary to evaluate whether the university's decision was entitled to immunity as a discretionary action, and the question before the district court was not judicial review of the termination decision but whether the contract allowed the university to proceed with the hearing. The district court did not abuse its discretion in denying a request to apply issue preclusion because the issues decided during the termination proceedings were not identical to those presented in the professor's district court complaint. Finally, the district court did not err by denying the university's motion for a directed verdict because the evidence presented was not so overwhelming that any other verdict would have been contrary to the law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment.

 

514 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Seres v. Lerner


No. 40938


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 928; 102 P.3d 91; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 127; 33 Media L. Rep. 1139; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 95



December 21, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court granting a motion to dismiss. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Brent T. Adams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the sister of a victim killed by respondent felon, sued respondent pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 217.007 on behalf of her mother after expiration of the statute of limitations for bringing a wrongful death action. The Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada) granted respondent's motion to dismiss. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, convicted of manslaughter, wrote a book about the crime. Appellant sued to recover the book proceeds. The trial court dismissed under a United States Supreme Court decision that voided a similar Son of Sam statute based upon First Amendment considerations. The trial court found a First Amendment violation despite determining the statute addressed compelling state interests in reimbursing crime victims and prohibiting profiteering from criminal activity. The parties raised issues concerning whether the statute involved state action, was content-based, and narrowly addressed compelling state interests. It was a content-based restriction on speech, the validity of which was subject to the strict scrutiny analysis. Although the measure addressed compelling state interests, it suffered from overinclusiveness because it regulated more speech than necessary to serve the state's interest; it allowed recovery of proceeds from works including expression both related and unrelated to the crime, imposing a disincentive to engage in public discourse and nonexploitative discussion of it. It violated the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

515 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Nev. Comm'n on Ethics v. Ballard


No. 41785


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 862; 102 P.3d 544; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 122; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 91



December 17, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order concluding that the Nevada Commission on Ethics lacked the power to (1) determine whether a political candidate's financial disclosure statement was adequate, or (2) seek fines against any of the respondents who timely filed a statement. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; William A. Maddox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and sanctions denied..


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The First Judicial District Court, Carson City, Nevada, granted judgment in favor of respondent political candidates by finding that appellant Nevada Commission on Ethics lacked the power to determine whether a candidate's financial disclosure statement was adequate, or seek fines against any of the candidates who timely filed a statement. The commission appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court noted that the commission's express statutory power to recover and waive or reduce penalties necessarily depended on the implicit power to determine whether a candidate's filing qualified as a financial disclosure statement. Provisions of the Ethics in Government Law vested the commission with the power to determine the adequacy of a candidate's financial disclosure statement. The commission had the power to seek the statutorily-accrued civil penalties against the candidates. The candidates' notices in lieu of statement of financial disclosure and related filings were so devoid of the necessary information that they could not be considered financial disclosure statements. Because the commission did not institute a civil action to recover the penalties while it had the statutory authority to do so, any such action now fell to the Secretary of State. The statutes were not vague and the candidates offered no evidence to show that the financial disclosure statutes had a chilling effect on the exercise of any First Amendment rights. Candidates were required to file financial disclosure statements only if they ran for public office, a purely voluntary act.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

516 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Horne v. City of Mesquite


No. 41705


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 700; 100 P.3d 168; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 102; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 79



November 10, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court declaratory judgment regarding the validity of citizen initiatives. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Michael L. Douglas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, entered a declaratory judgment regarding the validity of two initiative ordinances approved by respondent city. The district court determined that one of the ordinances was invalid, but it was severable. As to the second ordinance, the district court determined that it directly conflicted with a statute, and it was wholly invalid. Appellant mayor challenged the decision.


OVERVIEW: The first ordinance, Mesquite, Nev., Ordinance MQ1, governed public land sales, requiring the city to conduct all public land sales by a public auction or a public sealed bid process. The second ordinance, Mesquite, Nev., Ordinance MQ3, governed candidacy eligibility, requiring an elected official or public employee to file his resignation from office/employment before seeking election as mayor or city council member. The district court determined that the first ordinance was invalid, inasmuch as it conflicted with Nev. Rev. Stat. § 266.267(1), Nev. Rev. Stat. § 268.050, and Nev. Rev. Stat. § 277.050, but determined that the severability clause of MQ1 saved it from being wholly invalid. Yet, MQ1 essentially eliminated the portion of § 266.267(1) that allowed the city council to lease, sell, or exchange land at a price lower than the appraised price if it was in the best interest of the public. MQ1 would likely prevent the city council from ever exercising its discretion to sell land at less than its appraised value. MQ1 was repugnant to § 266.267(1); it was invalid. Both ordinances were impermissibly repugnant to provisions of Nev. Rev. Stat. ch. 266, and they were unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the district court's judgment in part and reverse in part.

Univ. & Cmty. College Sys. of Nev. v. Nevadans for Sound Gov't


No. 43652


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 712; 100 P.3d 179; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 106; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 81



November 10, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order granting a preliminary injunction in a matter involving the constitutional and statutory parameters of the right to access public property to collect signatures for ballot petitions. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Kenneth C. Cory, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a county transportation commission and a state university system, challenged a decision of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which issued a preliminary injunction enjoining appellants from interfering with respondent group's petition-circulating activities in violation of the First Amendment and Nev. Const. art. 1, § 9.


OVERVIEW: The group gathered signatures on a referendum petition and an initiative petition to be placed on a ballot. The group was denied access to commission and university property. The court held: (1) although Nev. Rev. Stat. § 293.127565 created an obligation for the commission and university to provide areas for signature gathering, commission and university properties were limited public forums, and their policies were reviewed under the reasonableness standard generally applied to time, place, and manner restrictions of limited public and nonpublic forums; (4) under the First Amendment, there was nothing inherently unreasonable in requiring a petition circulator to provide advance notice of his or her intended signature-gathering activities; (5) because the university building was occupied by a private party, § 293.127565 did not apply because it was only applied to buildings that were physically occupied by the government at the time in question; and (6) the commission regulations requiring a three-day notice and dictating that the designated area should be one that could be completely avoided by the public were unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that portion of the preliminary injunction relating to the commission to the extent that it related to the guidelines regarding notice, designated area, and signing a commission request form. The court reversed the injunction as to the university and the other commission guidelines.

SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 600; 97 P.3d 1140; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 87; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 68



September 16, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Nika v. State, 2004 Nev. LEXIS 143 (Nev., Dec. 16, 2004)

DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.

519 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Heller v. Give Nev. a Raise, Inc.


No. 43690


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 481; 96 P.3d 732; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 68; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 53



September 2, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court judgment requiring the Nevada Secretary of State to qualify previously discounted signatures on two ballot-initiative petitions. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; William A. Maddox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Nevada Secretary of State and two associations, sought review of a judgment from the First Judicial District Court, Carson City (Nevada), which, in an action brought by respondent initiative proponents, declared affidavit requirements for petition circulators to be unconstitutional and ordered the Secretary to qualify signatures that had been stricken from two initiative petitions and to place the initiatives on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Nev. Const. art. 19, § 3(1), the Secretary discounted thousands of signatures in booklets that lacked a valid affidavit signed by a registered voter who signed that particular booklet. The initiative proponents argued that the affidavit requirement violated the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, by burdening political speech. The court found merit in that argument, because the First Amendment precluded states from requiring petition circulators to be registered voters. Because Nev. Const. art. 19, § 2(2) permitted only registered voters to sign an initiative petition, a circulator could not authenticate signatures unless he or she was a registered voter. Under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 295.055(2), voters could sign a petition only in the county in which they were registered. Thus, only voters registered in the county of circulation could serve as affiants. Consequently, the court concluded that Nev. Const. art. 19, § 3(1) severely burdened political speech and that the burden was exacerbated by Nev. Rev. Stat. § 295.055(2). The affidavit requirement did not tangibly advance the State's interests in preventing corruption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment.

State v. Williams


No. 41109


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 473; 93 P.3d 1258; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 61; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 52



July 22, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Williams v. Nevada, 543 U.S. 958, 125 S. Ct. 409, 160 L. Ed. 2d 321, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 7154 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court granting a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Michael L. Douglas, Judge.
Williams v. State, 118 Nev. 536, 50 P.3d 1116, 2002 Nev. LEXIS 67 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving and/or being in actual physical control of a vehicle while under the influence of a controlled substance and/or with a prohibited substance in the blood or urine, reckless driving, and involuntary manslaughter. A jury before the Eight Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, convicted defendant. Defendant timely filed a post-conviction motion. The district court granted the motion. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Carboxylic acid, a metabolite, was found in defendant's blood. The district court had concluded that, because carboxylic acid was not listed in either Schedule I or Schedule II, it was not a prohibited substance under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 484.1245 or Nev. Rev. Stat. § 484.379(3). The State contended that the petition was procedurally barred pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 34.810. The supreme court agreed with the State that, because the claim was not raised earlier, the claim was barred. There was no merit to defendant's allegation of good cause. She presented no specific evidence to demonstrate or explain why this issue could not have been raised either at trial or on direct appeal. Further, while marijuana metabolite was not listed on either schedule, it was clear from the plain language of both Nev. Rev. Stat. § 484.379 and § 484.1245 that marijuana metabolite was a prohibited substance. The district court erred in granting the post-conviction petition.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the trial court's ruling.

Heller v. Legislature of Nev.


No. 43079


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 456; 93 P.3d 746; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 59; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 51



July 14, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original petition for a writ of mandamus seeking to compel the Legislature to fulfill its obligations under Article 3, Section 1(1), and Article 4, Section 6 of the Nevada Constitution.



DISPOSITION: Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Secretary of State sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondent Nevada Legislature to fulfill its obligations under Nev. Const. art. 3, § 1(1) and art. 4, § 6 as it pertained to executive branch employees serving in the Legislature. The Secretary sought an interpretation of Nev. Const. art. 3, § 1(1) and an order directing the Legislature to use that interpretation when qualifying its members under Nev. Const. art. 4, § 6.


OVERVIEW: The Secretary claimed that separation of powers barred any employee from serving in the executive branch of government and simultaneously serving as a member of the Legislature. The court held that the Secretary lacked standing to seek mandamus relief in order to exclude government employees from serving in the Legislature, as the Secretary had no discernible interest and only had standing to seek enforcement of election laws. The court found that the matter was not ripe for review, because the Secretary requested that relief apply on a prospective basis, and that, until executive branch employees were actually seated in the legislative session, there was no concrete controversy to resolve. The court also held that the Secretary sued the wrong party, because the authority to determine members' qualifications rested with each House, rather than the Legislature as a whole, thus the Secretary sought to compel an act that the Legislature as a whole had no legal authority to perform. The court concluded that, even if the Secretary had standing and sued the correct parties at the proper time, quo warranto, rather than mandamus, was the proper mechanism to try title to a public office.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Secretary of State's petition for a writ of mandamus.

Allred v. State


No. 40924


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 410; 92 P.3d 1246; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 54; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 47



July 12, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count of battery with substantial bodily harm. Seventh Judicial District Court, White Pine County; Steve L. Dobrescu, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of battery with substantial bodily harm in the Seventh Judicial District Court of White Pine County, Nevada. Defendant appealed, asserting that two erroneous written jury instructions were allowed into the jury's deliberations, jurors were improperly allowed to question witnesses, the prosecutor improperly commented on his failure to testify, and that there was insufficient evidence to sustain his conviction.


OVERVIEW: The victim and defendant were at a night club. The victim got into an argument with one witness. However, it was defendant who followed the victim outside during the argument. Defendant later gave inconsistent statements to a deputy, first asserting he had simply broken up a fight between the victim and the aforementioned witness, and then admitting that he was alone with the victim at the time of the fight. The victim was severely injured, such that he required plastic surgery and the installation of titanium plates in his face. The appellate court held: (1) the trial court's clerical error in allowing the two erroneous jury instructions into the jury's deliberations was harmless, given its instruction to disregard same; (2) the trial court did not err in allowing jurors to present written questions to the witnesses after the trial court approved those questions; (3) the prosecutor did not comment on defendant's failure to testify by stating defendant did not present an accurate account of how the assault occurred; (4) substantial evidence supported defendant's conviction, and (5) defendant's 60 month sentence, within the statutory guidelines, was not cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed.

Vincent L.G. v. State Div. of Child & Family Servs., Dep't of Human Res. (In re D.R.H.)


No. 41352


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 422; 92 P.3d 1230; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 55; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 48



July 12, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order terminating appellants' parental rights. Seventh Judicial District Court, White Pine County; Dan L. Papez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Seventh Judicial District Court, White Pine County, Nevada, terminated the parental rights of appellant mother and father to their three children. The mother and father appealed, arguing that clear and convincing evidence did not support the termination of their parental rights. The father also contended that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 128.109(2) was unconstitutional because it infringed on his substantive due process rights.


OVERVIEW: At the time of the termination hearing, the children had been in foster care for over 30 months. Therefore, pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 128.109(2), it was presumed that their best interest was served by parental termination. The supreme court held that § 128.109(2) was constitutional, as it was narrowly tailored to serve the State's compelling interest in seeking permanent placement for children rather than having them remain in foster care. The trial court found that the parents were unfit, had made only token efforts to regain custody, and that terminating their parental rights was in the children's best interests. The supreme court found that substantial evidence supported these findings: (1) the mother failed to overcome her drug problems; (2) despite going to anger management and domestic violence courses, the father failed to control his temper; (3) he had three domestic violence convictions and was incarcerated at the time of the hearing; (4) he failed to communicate with the children for almost two years; (5) the children were present during several episodes of domestic violence between the parents; and (6) the children made positive adjustments while in foster care.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Browning v. State


No. 39063


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 347; 91 P.3d 39; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 45; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 39



June 10, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Post-conviction relief denied at Browning v. State, 2008 Nev. LEXIS 61 (Nev., July 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying a petition for post-conviction relief. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph S. Pavlikowski, Judge.
Browning v. State, 104 Nev. 269, 757 P.2d 351, 1988 Nev. LEXIS 35 (1988)



DISPOSITION: Motion for post-conviction relief granted in part and denied in part. Defendant's death sentence vacated; case remanded for new penalty hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate was convicted of murder. The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, entered an order denying a petition for post-conviction relief. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The victim was stabbed to death during a robbery. The victim described his assailant. Another witness also identified a man he saw fleeing. A third witness described how she helped the inmate. During sentencing, the jury found aggravating circumstances. Appellate counsel, however, was ineffective for failing to challenge the aggravating circumstance of depravity of mind, as the jury instructions failed to provide the constitutionally required guidance to jurors. The inmate claimed that the prosecutor withheld information regarding benefits given to an important witness. Nevertheless, the outcome would not have been different had this information been disclosed. The inmate also contended that the State withheld the fact that shoeprints near the victim were already present when the first police officer arrived at the crime scene. This fact, however, was not material in determining whether trial counsel was ineffective. The inmate also claimed that the State committed a Brady violation. The challenged information, though, was readily available to the defense. As to whether key evidence was not preserved, the inmate failed to show bad faith, or that the evidence was exculpatory.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated the death sentence, and it remanded the matter for a new penalty hearing. In all other respects, the supreme court affirmed the order denying post-conviction relief.

 

525 of 673 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Office of the AG v. NOS Communs., Inc.


No. 40348


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 65; 84 P.3d 1052; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 11; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 11



February 25, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order denying injunctive relief. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; Michael R. Griffin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The First Judicial District Court, Carson City, Nevada, denied the motion of appellant Office of the Attorney General, Division of Consumer Protection (BCP) for a preliminary injunction against respondent telecommunication companies seeking to enjoin the companies from engaging in allegedly deceptive trade practices. The BCP appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court denied the BCP injunctive relief, concluding that an existing administrative hearing was an adequate remedy at law. The appellate court found that because the BCP did not assert an affirmative claim for injunctive relief in its answer or through a counterclaim and did not attach affidavits containing admissible statements or admissible documents to the motion, the motion for preliminary injunction was procedurally defective. Therefore, the district court did not abuse its discretion by denying the BCP injunctive relief. However, the appellate court addressed the merits of the district court's ruling. The appellate court determined that, by considering whether the BCP had an adequate legal remedy, the district court erred because equitable considerations, such as irreparable harm and an inadequate legal remedy, were presumed in a statutory enforcement action. The only issue before the district court was whether the BCP presented admissible evidence establishing a reasonable likelihood that the companies were engaging in deceptive trade practices. Once such a showing was made, an injunction should issue.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

526 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Thomas v. State


No. 40248


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


120 Nev. 37; 83 P.3d 818; 2004 Nev. LEXIS 7; 120 Nev. Adv. Rep. 7



February 10, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, En banc Thomas v. State, 122 Nev. 1361, 148 P.3d 727, 2006 Nev. LEXIS 147 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Sally L. Loehrer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's convictions for two counts of first-degree murder and four other felonies, as well as his two death sentences, were affirmed on appeal. Defendant filed a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The Eighth Judicial District Court of Clark County, Nevada, denied the petition and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant raised several issues alleging his counsel was ineffective and asserted that the trial court erred in holding an evidentiary hearing on only some of his claims rather than all of them. The supreme court agreed that defendant's trial counsel was ineffective for failing to object to a Petrocelli instruction. Further, there was a reasonable probability that jurors mistakenly believed that defendant could eventually receive parole even if they returned sentences of life in prison without parole and that this belief contributed to their decision to render verdicts of death. As such, a new penalty hearing was required. Trial counsel was not ineffective for failing to object to victim impact evidence and a jury instruction regarding premeditation. The prosecutor's comment that there was no program that rehabilitated killers and that defendant was deserving of the same sympathy, compassion, and mercy that he extended to the victims were improper and trial counsel should have objected to them. Defendant's remaining claims were procedurally barred.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed but his death sentences were reversed and remanded to the trial court for a new penalty hearing.

Clem v. State


No. 40008, No. 40009, No. 40028


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 615; 81 P.3d 521; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 81; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 64



December 30, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Clem v. State, 91 P.3d 35, 2004 Nev. LEXIS 41 (2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Clem v. Nev., 543 U.S. 947, 125 S. Ct. 358, 160 L. Ed. 2d 263, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 7032 (U.S., Oct. 18, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Consolidated appeals from orders of the district court denying post-conviction petitions for writs of habeas corpus. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Lee A. Gates, Judge.
Bridgewater v. Hardison, 1996 U.S. App. LEXIS 29127 (9th Cir. Nev., Nov. 5, 1996)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state inmates who used electrical household items to torture their kidnap victims, challenged the sentence enhancements imposed upon them for the use of "deadly weapons," on the ground that post-conviction judicial rulings and amendments altered the definition of deadly weapons under Nev. Rev. Stat. 193.165 to exclude household items, in appeals from orders of the Eighth Judicial District Court (Nevada) denying them habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Appellants argued that the retroactivity rules applicable to decisions interpreting federal criminal statutes and decisions announcing constitutional rules of criminal procedure required the retroactive application of the definition of deadly weapons should not include a red-hot table fork and heated electric iron, as those were not weapons that could readily cause death. At the time of appellants' crimes, Nev. Rev. Stat. 193.165 did not define "deadly weapon," but required imposition of a sentence equal and consecutive to the term of imprisonment prescribed by statute for the crime being committed with the use of a firearm or other deadly weapon. The court held that the appellants could not avoid the law of the case, which upheld the validity of their sentence enhancements, and that appellants failed to overcome the procedural bars of Nev. Rev. Stat. 34.726 and 34.810 as to untimely and subsequent petitions. The applicable state and federal precedent required limited retroactivity on collateral review, but neither upset the usual rule of non-retroactivity for rules that carried no constitutional significance.


OUTCOME: The denial of habeas corpus relief was affirmed, as the appellants' petitions were procedurally barred.

State v. Bennett


No. 38934


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 589; 81 P.3d 1; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 83; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 63



December 30, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of mandamus granted Bennett v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court of Nev., 121 P.3d 605, 2005 Nev. LEXIS 94 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal and cross-appeal from an order of the district court granting in part and denying in part a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus in a capital case. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; John S. McGroarty, Judge.
Bennett v. State, 106 Nev. 135, 787 P.2d 797, 1990 Nev. LEXIS 21 (1990)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death. Defendant unsuccessfully sought relief on direct appeal and in a prior post-conviction proceeding. In his second post-conviction proceeding, the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), granted defendant's petition in part by vacating his death sentence and granting a new hearing. The State appealed, and defendant cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his accomplice attempted to rob a clerk at a convenience store, and defendant shot and killed the clerk. At the conclusion of the penalty hearing, the jury determined that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances and rendered a verdict of death. Defendant raised his claim in an untimely and successive post-conviction habeas petition. Nevertheless, the court concluded that the finding of the improper aggravator in this case, combined with the prejudicial impact of Brady violations so undermined the reliability of the jury's sentencing determination that the application of procedural bars to preclude consideration of defendant's claim would amount to a fundamental miscarriage of justice. The facts of this case did not support the finding aggravator that defendant killed the store clerk at random and without apparent motive; the state showed only that defendant unnecessarily killed the clerk in connection with the attempted robbery. The court was unable to conclude beyond a reasonable doubt that the jury would have returned a sentence of death without the aggravating circumstance and in the absence of the State's Brady violations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's order vacating defendant's sentence of death and ordering a new penalty hearing.

Harris Assocs. v. Clark County Sch. Dist.


No. 38140


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 638; 81 P.3d 532; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 85; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 66



December 30, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying a motion to compel arbitration. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Stephen L. Huffaker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractor appealed from an order of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), that denied the contractor's motion to compel arbitration pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 338.150(1), of its dispute with defendant school district, which asserted it was entitled to a jury trial of the claims in issue.


OVERVIEW: The contractor asserted that the meaning of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 338.150(1) mandated arbitration as the means to resolve disputes that arise in public works construction projects. Even if the language of the statute was not clear and unambiguous, the court noted that the legislature had previously changed the language concerning arbitration from "may" to "shall," which strongly indicated that the legislature intended arbitration to be mandatory for any non-exempted agency. The school district's constitutional and procedural rights to a jury trial had thus been waived by the legislature.


OUTCOME: The order of the district court was reversed and remanded.

 

530 of 673 DOCUMENTS


T.R. v. State (In re T.R.)


No. 38480


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 646; 80 P.3d 1276; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 86; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 67



December 30, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order adjudicating a juvenile delinquent for sexual assault of a victim under the age of fourteen. Eighth Judicial District Court, Family Court Division, Clark County; Cynthia Dianne Steel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile challenged an order of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Family Court Division, Clark County (Nevada), adjudicating him delinquent for sexual assault of a victim under the age of 14 in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 200.366(1) and subjecting him to a hearing after he turned 21-years-old under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 62.590 to determine if he should comply with adult sex offender registration.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile was adjudicated delinquent for sodomizing his four-year-old victim, and was ordered to undergo a hearing when he reached 21-one-years of age to determine whether he should be required to comply with adult sex offender registration statutes. On appeal, the court determined that § 62.590 was void for vagueness and reversed the portion of the order requiring the juvenile to undergo the hearing. Section 62.590 provided no guidance with respect to how a child should conduct himself to avoid lifetime sex offender registration; a child could successfully complete counseling and substance abuse treatment programs but because of § 62.590's express language could still face registration, given the lack of standards for a court to use in determining whether a child is rehabilitated or poses a continuing threat to society. Because § 62.590 provided no guidance for the child to "act accordingly" and authorized arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement, it was unconstitutional. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to adjudicate the juvenile delinquent for sexual abuse given the victim's grandmother's admissible testimony of what he told her about the assault.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed insofar as it adjudicated the juvenile delinquent. The order was reversed insofar as it required the juvenile to submit to a hearing to determine whether he should comply with adult sex offender registration and notification statutes.

OUTCOME: The petition was dismissed, and the opinion was clarified.

The court emphasized that two aggravating circumstances existed and that defendant had had an extensive, ongoing criminal history, including another strangulation murder of a female victim. City of Las Vegas Downtown Redevelopment Agency v. Pappas

SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 429; 76 P.3d 1; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 64; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 51



September 8, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Pappas v. Las Vegas Downtown Redev. Agency, 541 U.S. 912, 124 S. Ct. 1603, 158 L. Ed. 2d 260, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 1990 (U.S., Mar. 8, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal and cross-appeal from a district court judgment in an eminent domain action. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Don P. Chairez, Judge, and Stephen L. Huffaker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a city redevelopment agency and an anchor project, sought review of an order of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which granted respondent property owners' motion to dismiss the agency's eminent domain complaint. The owners filed a cross-appeal from an order dismissing their counterclaims.


OVERVIEW: The agency identified various physical, economic, and social conditions within the area constituting "blight" under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 279.388. These conditions included increased crime rates and requirements for police assistance, business flight from the area, decline in tourism, lack of parking, perceptions of lack of safety, and increases in vacant and aging buildings. The court initially rejected appellants' estoppel claim but held that for all future eminent domain cases, challenges to a public taking had to be raised prior to occupancy and material demolition, alteration, or construction on the property. The court found that there was a rational relationship to justify the exercise of eminent domain. The agency's purpose for entering into the redevelopment agreement was to construct a safe entertainment street mall to attract visitors and business back to the downtown area. The project's central focus was to combat economic, social, and physical blight, a public purpose, not to service a single business. Even if the plan had been challenged within the 90-day period, as provided by Nev. Rev. Stat. § 279.609, substantial evidence supported the agency's determination of blight.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the district court dismissing the complaint in eminent domain and that portion of the district court's subsequent order pertaining to the lost rent claims. The court remanded the matter to the district court for further proceedings. The court affirmed the remaining portion of the district court's order dismissing the counterclaims.

State v. Haberstroh


No. 38404


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 173; 69 P.3d 676; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 27; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 23

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] These are consolidated appeals from an order of the district court granting in part and denying in part a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus in a capital case. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County. Michael L. Douglas, Judge.
Haberstroh v. State, 105 Nev. 739, 782 P.2d 1343, 1989 Nev. LEXIS 298 (1989)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a second postconviction proceeding, the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, granted partial relief to respondent petitioner, who sought a writ of habeas corpus. The district court vacated petitioner's death sentence and granted him a new penalty hearing. The State appealed from that part of the order vacating petitioner's sentence, and petitioner appealed from the part denying the remainder of his petition.


OVERVIEW: The district court concluded that petitioner's death sentence should be vacated because the jury's finding of depravity of mind as an aggravating circumstance, without a proper limiting instruction on depravity of mind, was unconstitutional. The weight of the other aggravating circumstances was not so great that the instant court was convinced beyond a reasonable doubt that the jury would have returned a death sentence even without the invalid aggravator, especially considering the emphasis the State placed on depravity.


OUTCOME: The instant court affirmed the district court's order vacating petitioner's death sentence and granting a new penalty hearing. Because the other issues raised by petitioner did not warrant relief, the instant court also affirmed the district court's order insofar as it otherwise upheld petitioner's conviction.

Sanders v. State


No. 39702


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 135; 67 P.3d 323; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 17; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 17



April 28, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Sanders v. State, 2003 Nev. LEXIS 60 (Nev., Aug. 29, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of conviction for felony nonsupport. Fifth Judicial District Court, Nye County; Robert W. Lane, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury of the Fifth Judicial District Court, Nye County, Nevada, convicted defendant of felony nonsupport, in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.020. Defendant appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendant maintained that he was unable to pay child support while incarcerated. On appeal, he argued that: (1) the "without good cause" language in Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.051 was vague, thereby making the affirmative defense statute unconstitutional; (2) the State misinterpreted Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.020, thereby miscalculating his arrearages; and (3) insufficient evidence supported his conviction, given that he did not have significant ability or opportunity to pay child support while incarcerated. The instant court concluded that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.051 was not vague and was constitutional. Moreover, because Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.051 was an affirmative defense statute, it did not encourage arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement. Defendant accumulated arrearages in excess of $ 10,000 by failing to make a single child support payment from July 1998 to April 2001. Accordingly, the State properly charged defendant with violating Nev. Rev. Stat. § 201.020. Although incarceration could be asserted as an affirmative defense, sufficient evidence supported defendant's conviction because the jury had good reason to reject his affirmative defense of incarceration.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Kirkpatrick v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 37593


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


119 Nev. 66; 64 P.3d 1056; 2003 Nev. LEXIS 10; 119 Nev. Adv. Rep. 8



March 14, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for rehearing of Kirkpatrick v. District Court, 118 Nev. 233, 43 P.3d 998 (2002). Original petition for a writ of mandamus seeking to compel the district court to vacate an order issuing a marriage license allowing petitioner's fifteen-year-old daughter to marry and seeking to annul the marriage.
Kirkpatrick v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court, 118 Nev. 233, 43 P.3d 998, 2002 Nev. LEXIS 36 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Petition for rehearing granted; previous opinion withdrawn and this one substituted; petition for mandamus denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father petitioned for a writ of mandamus to respondents, judge and Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, seeking to compel the district court to vacate an order issuing a marriage license allowing the father's 15-year-old daughter to marry and seeking to annul the marriage.


OVERVIEW: Under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 122.025, a minor under the age of 16 was allowed to marry with the consent of 1 parent and district court authorization. This allowed the father's 15-year-old daughter to marry a 48-year-old man with the consent of her mother. The father argued that the statute was unconstitutional. The appellate court found that the right to marry was a fundamental right. The statute provided a safeguard against an erroneous marriage decision by the minor and the consenting parent, by giving the district court the discretion to withhold authorization if it found that there were no extraordinary circumstances and/or the proposed marriage was not in the minor's best interest. The father lost his right to exercise legal control over his daughter during her minority. He still had the other legal and social attributes of parenthood. The state had an interest in fostering appropriate marriages and tailoring its statutes in such a way as to take into account the individual variations in maturity, rather than just setting an arbitrary rule of age. The father had no standing to annul his daughter's marriage.


OUTCOME: The petition for mandamus was denied.

Reel v. Harrison


No. 36529


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 881; 60 P.3d 480; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 106; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 89



December 26, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order granting a petition to remove minor child from the state. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; Michael E. Fondi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father appealed an order of the First Judicial District Court, Carson City, Nevada, permitting respondent mother to remove their minor child from the state, pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 125C.200.


OVERVIEW: The trial court determined that § 125C.200 violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment by restricting a custodial parent's fundamental right to travel. The trial court also concluded that even if § 125C.200 was constitutional, the mother was still permitted to relocate the minor child to New Jersey, based on the career and educational opportunities available there, the presence of the child's extended family in New Jersey and the ability to maintain reasonable visitation with the father. The father objected to the move because he believed that removing the child from the stability of regular visitation with him was not in the child's best interest. The instant court concluded that § 125C.200 did not violate the United States Constitution and that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in granting the mother's petition to relocate. The trial court erred in engaging in an equal protection analysis. Substantial evidence in the record supported the trial court's findings that permitting the mother to remove the child from the state was in the child's best interest.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was affirmed.

 

539 of 673 DOCUMENTS


City of Las Vegas v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 38582


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 859; 59 P.3d 477; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 99; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 87



December 20, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original petition for a writ of certiorari or mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city filed a criminal complaint charging defendant with one count of annoying a minor pursuant to former Nev. Rev. Stat. § 207.260. The Nevada District Court ruled that former § 207.260 was unconstitutionally vague. The city petitioned for extraordinary relief.


OVERVIEW: On review, the city argued that the trial court erred determining that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 270.260 was unconstitutionally vague. The supreme court found that the criminal complaint merely charged that defendant had willfully and unlawfully "annoyed" a minor; it did not allege that defendant had "molested" a minor. The language of the statute did not specify what type of annoying behavior was prohibited, nor did it define the term "molest." By its terms, the statute was not limited only to annoyances of a sexual nature, and it provided no indication of whether the perpetrator must subjectively intend to annoy the minor, or if mere unintentional, bothersome conduct, in and of itself, was sufficient to subject an individual to criminal sanctions. Further, because the statute failed to adequately set forth the conduct proscribed, it provided those charged with enforcement of its provisions unfettered and unguided discretion to decide what annoying activity fell within its parameters. Accordingly, the supreme court held that the statute was facially invalid because it: (1) failed to provide fair notice of the prohibited conduct; and (2) authorized and encouraged arbitrary enforcement.


OUTCOME: The city's petition was denied.

 

540 of 673 DOCUMENTS


O'Guinn v. State


No. 37300, No. 38382


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 849; 59 P.3d 488; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 100; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 85



December 20, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Consolidated appeals from judgments of conviction seeking relief to withdraw guilty pleas. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Jeffrey D. Sobel, Judge (Docket No. 37300). Seventh Judicial District Court, White Pine County; Dan L. Papez, Judge (Docket No. 38382).
O'Guinn v. State, 2001 Nev. LEXIS 1552 (Nev., Feb. 9, 2001)



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: As part of a plea agreement, one defendant entered a plea of guilty but mentally ill in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), to two counts of burglary, one count of open and gross lewdness, and two counts of sexual assault on a victim 65 years of age or older. The second defendant pled guilty to an amended information charging him with battery with intent to kill with the use of a deadly weapon. Both defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants presented the issue as to whether the supreme court's decision in Finger v. State automatically entitled a defendant convicted pursuant to a guilty but mentally ill plea to withdraw that plea and proceed to trial. One defendant, relying on the supreme court's decision in Finger, sought to withdraw his plea, enter a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity, and proceed to trial. The second defendant argued his case should be remanded to the trial court to allow him to withdraw his plea of guilty but mentally ill, enter a plea of not guilty, and proceed to trial. Both defendants based their appeal on the premise that mental illness prevented them from understanding the consequences of the plea. The statutory scheme under which defendants entered their pleas was unconstitutional and unenforceable. Therefore, the supreme court held that both of the cases must be remanded to the trial court and defendants be provided an opportunity to enter new pleas.


OUTCOME: Both of the cases were remanded to the trial court where defendants were be provided an opportunity to enter new pleas. On remand, the State would not be bound by any plea negotiations previously agreed to by the parties and the original charges in both cases may be reinstated.

 

541 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Sheriff v. Burdg


No. 38105, No. 38264


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 853; 59 P.3d 484; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 101; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 86



December 20, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Sheriff v. Burdg, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 4450 (U.S., June 9, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Consolidated appeals from district court orders concluding that NRS 453.322(1)(b) is unconstitutional. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; James W. Hardesty, Judge (No. 38105); Brent T. Adams, Judge (No. 38264).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The underlying actions involved separate cases in which defendants were charged with possession of a majority of the ingredients required to manufacture a controlled substance in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.322(1)(b). In both cases, the Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County, Nevada, ruled that the statute was void for vagueness and, therefore, unconstitutional. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the State challenged the trial court's rulings on two grounds: (1) the trial court did not determine whether the statute was vague as applied to defendants (in both cases); and (2) the trial court failed to address whether the statute was vague in all of its applications. The supreme court agreed with the trial court, finding that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.322(1)(b) was facially vague because it infringed on constitutionally protected conduct, was incapable of any valid applications, failed to provide sufficient notice of the prohibited conduct, and encouraged arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement. The statute contained no intent element, yet imposed criminal sanctions on what was otherwise non-criminal activity. Further, the statute failed to list the items that might be described as "ingredients" required to manufacture or compound a controlled substance. Moreover, the absence of an intent element and the ambiguities regarding the required ingredients allowed arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement. Thus, the statute, as written, failed to provide law enforcement with adequate guidance concerning the precise scope of activities it aspires to proscribe.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

542 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Hiibel v. Sixth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 38876


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 868; 59 P.3d 1201; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 102; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 88



December 20, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, Motion granted by Hiibel v. Sixth Judicial Dist. Court, 157 L. Ed. 2d 309, 124 S. Ct. 430, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 7710 (U.S., 2003)
Motion granted by Hiibel v. Sixth Judicial Dist. Court, 158 L. Ed. 2d 75, 124 S. Ct. 1494, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 1026 (U.S., 2004)
Affirmed by Hiibel v. Sixth Judicial Dist. Court, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 4385 (U.S., June 21, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original petition for a writ of certiorari challenging the district court's order affirming petitioner's conviction in justice court for resisting or obstructing an officer investigating a crime.



DISPOSITION: Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of resisting a public officer when he refused to provide identification to a police officer. On appeal, the Nevada District Court held it was reasonable and necessary for the officer to request identification from defendant, and affirmed defendant's conviction. Defendant petitioned for a writ of certiorari, challenging the constitutionality of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 171.123(3).


OVERVIEW: On review, the supreme court determined that the issue of the constitutionality of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 171.123(3) presented an issue of first impression. In the instant case, an officer responded to a concerned citizen's call regarding an observed assault. The officer approached defendant and requested identification after suspecting that defendant had engaged in driving under the influence and domestic violence against his minor daughter. Defendant refused 11 times to identify himself and instead placed his hands behind his back and challenged the officer to take him to jail. The supreme court determined that an ordinary person would conclude it was defendant who was unreasonable, not the law. Further, § 171.123(3) was narrowly written as it applied only in situations where an officer had an articulable suspicion that a person was engaged in criminal behavior. Requiring defendant, who was reasonably suspected of committing a crime, to identify himself was a commonsense requirement to protect both the public and the officer. Thus, the supreme court concluded that § 171.123(3) was good law consistent with the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's petition for writ of certiorari was denied.

 

543 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Malecon Tobacco, LLC v. State ex rel. Dep't of Taxation


No. 38101


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 837; 59 P.3d 474; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 97; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 83



December 19, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order granting a motion to dismiss. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; William A. Maddox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. John S. Bartlett, Carson City, for Appellants. Frankie Sue Del Papa, Attorney General, Norman J. Azevedo, Chief Deputy Attorney General, and Joshua J. Hicks, Deputy Attorney General, Carson City, for Respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers brought a class action lawsuit in the First Judicial District Court, Carson City (Nevada), contesting the assessment of certain taxes. The trial court granted respondent Department of Taxation's motion to dismiss the taxpayers' complaint, based on lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court decided that two administrative remedies existed: (1) seeking a refund for illegally collected taxes, or (2) seeking an advisory opinion from the department regarding the constitutionality of the statutes upon which the taxes were based. The district court determined that the taxpayers should have exhausted those remedies before seeking relief in the district court. The taxpayers challenged the constitutionality of the statutes as applied to them. Resolution of the taxpayers' constitutional challenges hinged upon factual determinations. The constitutionality of the statutes challenged here, as applied, involved a factual evaluation, best left to the Department of Taxation, which could utilize its specialized skill and knowledge to inquire into the facts of the case. Since the taxpayers did not exhaust their administrative remedies, the district court properly dismissed the complaint for lack of subject matter jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

544 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Leslie v. Warden


No. 36546


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 773; 59 P.3d 440; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 96; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 78



December 18, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court denying a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Sally L. Loehrer, Judge.
Leslie v. State, 114 Nev. 8, 952 P.2d 966, 1998 Nev. LEXIS 12 (1998)



DISPOSITION: Sentence vacated and matter remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged an order from the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which denied his post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging his sentence of death for the crimes of burglary, robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, and first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner robbed a convenience store and fatally shot the clerk. Petitioner was convicted of burglary, robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, and first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon. The jury found four aggravating circumstances that it concluded outweighed the mitigating circumstance that petitioner had no significant criminal history, and imposed a sentence of death. The court affirmed petitioner's conviction and sentence. Petitioner filed a timely post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The district court denied petitioner's petition. On appeal, the court vacated the sentence and remanded. Petitioner received constitutionally effective assistance of trial and appellate counsel. He was not entitled to relief because of alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. While the facts supported the robbery and burglary sentencing aggravators, the facts did not support the jury finding that petitioner killed the clerk at random and without apparent motive. It was improperly based upon the fact that the killing was unnecessary to complete the robbery. The court could not say that the jury would have imposed death in the absence of the erroneous aggravators.


OUTCOME: The court vacated petitioner's sentence of death and remand the case for a new penalty hearing.

Johnson v. State


No. 36991


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 787; 59 P.3d 450; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 92; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 79



December 18, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by State v. Dist. Court, 121 Nev. 1175, 152 P.3d 810, 2005 Nev. LEXIS 1594 (Apr. 13, 2005)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by Johnson v. State, 122 Nev. 1344, 148 P.3d 767, 2006 Nev. LEXIS 146 (Dec. 28, 2006)
Petition denied by Johnson v. State, 124 Nev. 1482, 238 P.3d 828, 2008 Nev. LEXIS 1621 (May 6, 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Johnson v. State, 2017 Nev. LEXIS 100 (Oct. 5, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] This is an appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of four counts each of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon, robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, and first-degree kidnapping with the use of a deadly weapon, one count of burglary with the use of a deadly weapon, and a sentence of death. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Jeffrey D. Sobel, Judge.
Johnson v. Dist. Ct, 116 Nev. 1382, 62 P.3d 1168, 2000 Nev. LEXIS 1550 (June 8, 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, vacated in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, (Nevada) of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon, robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, first-degree kidnapping with the use of a deadly weapon, and one count of burglary with the use of a deadly weapon. Defendant was sentenced to death by a three-judge panel. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that his sentence violated the Sixth Amendment because, after the jury was unable to agree on a sentence, he was sentenced to death by a three-judge panel as allowed under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 175.556(1), which was unconstitutional. The supreme court agreed. Even if the guilty verdicts necessarily entailed the jury's finding of the two aggravators found by the three-judge panel, the guilt-phase verdicts did not and could not have entailed the required consideration of mitigating evidence. That evidence and that consideration were not presented to the jury until the penalty phase. The facts from the first penalty hearing did not establish harmless error either. Even assuming that the jury forms were proof of the jurors' findings, there was no verdict form or other evidence showing that the jurors unanimously agreed that the mitigating circumstances did not outweigh the aggravating circumstances, making defendant eligible for death. Therefore, the death sentence was unconstitutionally imposed on defendant.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed. The death sentence was reversed and the case was remanded for re-sentencing.

Garvin v. Ninth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 40144


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 749; 59 P.3d 1180; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 90; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 76



December 17, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Rehearing Denied February 21, 2003. Released for Publication February 25, 2003.
Related proceeding at Sustainable Growth Initiative Comm. v. Jumpers, LLC, 2006 Nev. LEXIS 9 (2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Real party in interest developer filed an action to enjoin real party in interest board of county commissioners (board) from placing petitioner residents' sustainable growth initiative on the ballot. The district court, Nevada, granted the injunction, but the district court's order was stayed, and the initiative was placed on the ballot and passed.


OVERVIEW: The residents submitted a sustainable growth initiative to the board to be placed on the November 2002 ballot. The district court granted the developer's injunction and ordered that the initiative not be placed on the ballot, but that order was stayed by the instant court. The district court noted that it based its grant of the injunction on Forman v. Eagle Thrifty Drugs & Markets, 89 Nev. 533, 516 P.2d 1234 (1973). The instant court reviewed that law that Foreman was based on and determined it was fundamentally flawed. The instant court overruled Foreman to the extent it held that: (1) the initiative power did not extend to the zoning processes of counties and cities, or other matters legislatively delegated to local governments; (2) due process requirements of notice and hearing applied to general zoning legislation by initiative; and (3) all changes to established zoning policies were administrative in nature. Finally, the instant court held that the sustainable growth initiative was legislative in nature and was properly submitted to the voters on November 2002 ballot.


OUTCOME: The stay on the injunction was vacated and the petition was granted. Further, a writ of mandamus was issued directing the district court to vacate its order granting the injunction in its entirety.

Dzul v. State


No. 37880


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 681; 56 P.3d 875; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 85; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 71



October 31, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied December 27, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of conviction entered pursuant to a guilty plea on one count of attempted lewdness with a child under the age of fourteen years. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Nancy M. Saitta, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, convicted defendant, on a guilty plea, to one count of attempted lewdness with a child under the age of 14 years. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the district court erred in considering the psychosexual evaluations because he was not Mirandized before the clinical interview portion of the evaluations. The appellate court found that nothing indicated that defendant objected or refused to submit to the psychosexual evaluation when he was interviewed. Nev. Rev. Stat. § 176A.110 prohibited the district court from placing defendant on probation unless the person who conducted the evaluation certified that defendant was not a menace to the health, safety, or morals of others. Defendant never asserted his Fifth Amendment rights during the clinical interviews nor did he refuse to participate in the evaluations. Probation was a form of leniency and defendant was not penalized for refusing to admit guilt to the underlying offense. Presenting defendant with the choice between admitting responsibility for the offense to which he pleaded guilty and increasing the likelihood of receiving a favorable psychosexual evaluation, or denying responsibility for the offense and reducing the likelihood of a favorable psychosexual evaluation did not violate the Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Citizens for a Pub. Train Trench Vote v. City of Reno


No. 39898


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 574; 53 P.3d 387; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 73; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 60



September 6, 2002, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a district court order permanently enjoining the Washoe County Registrar of Voters from placing on the ballot an initiative prohibiting the construction of a depressed trainway within the existing railroad right of way through Reno. DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the citizens, filed a petition for judicial review, writ of prohibition, or injunctive relief to stop the city from issuing certain bonds. Respondent city moved to dismiss and filed a counterclaim for declaratory judgment. A political action committee also sought declaratory relief. The cases were consolidated. The Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County, Nevada, entered an order against the citizens, who appealed.

The district court entered a written order declaring the initiative unconstitutional and enjoined the registrar of voters from placing the initiative on any city ballot. The citizens appealed. The supreme court held en banc that the initiative concerned an administrative matter, which exceeded the electorate's initiative power, and that the district court properly enjoined its inclusion on the ballot.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Hernandez v. State


No. 36859


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 513; 50 P.3d 1100; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 69; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 55



August 2, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied September 5, 2002. Writ of certiorari denied: Hernandez v. Nevada, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 1239 (U.S. Feb. 24, 2003).
Writ of certiorari denied Hernandez v. Nevada, 537 U.S. 1197, 123 S. Ct. 1263, 154 L. Ed. 2d 1034, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 1239 (2003)
Post-conviction relief denied at Hernandez v. State, 194 P.3d 1235, 2008 Nev. LEXIS 100 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of burglary while in possession of a weapon, first-degree murder with use of a deadly weapon, second-degree kidnapping, and unlawful sexual penetration of a dead human body, and from a sentence of death. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Sally L. Loehrer, Judge.
Hernandez v. State, 117 Nev. 463, 24 P.3d 767, 2001 Nev. LEXIS 42 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of, among other things, first-degree murder for stabbing his ex-wife to death and second-degree kidnapping for taking their three-year-old daughter immediately after the murder. Defendant appealed his conviction and claimed that his conviction was invalid due to numerous errors made by the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada.


OVERVIEW: Defendant brutally stabbed his ex-wife in front of their young daughter after having threatened to kill her on many occasions; the ex-wife had a protective order against him. Defendant took the child after the murder. Defendant claimed he could not kidnap his own daughter. During trial, the trial court sustained defendant's objections to the State's comments that the victim's family could no longer vacation with her and that the State disagreed with a defense witness. The appellate court held that defendant's taking of his daughter was unlawful, as he violated a protective order, a custody decree, and criminal statutes when he murdered the ex-wife; his status as sole surviving parent of the child did not render his seizure of the child lawful. The court also held that while the State's comments were improper, neither so infected either the guilt or penalty phase with unfairness as to deny defendant due process. Finally, the death sentence was held not to be excessive even though many mitigating factors existed, as defendant stalked his wife, murdered her without provocation in a horrific, savage manner; and did so in the presence of her, and his own, young daughter.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and sentence of death were affirmed.

Williams v. State


No. 37785


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 536; 50 P.3d 1116; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 67; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 56



August 2, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Writ of certiorari denied: Williams v. Nevada, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 8507 (U.S. Nov. 18, 2002).
Writ of certiorari denied Williams v. Nevada, 537 U.S. 1031, 123 S. Ct. 569, 154 L. Ed. 2d 446, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 8507 (2002)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Williams, 120 Nev. 473, 93 P.3d 1258, 2004 Nev. LEXIS 61 (2004)
Post-conviction relief denied at Williams v. Warden, 422 F.3d 1006, 2005 U.S. App. LEXIS 19265 (9th Cir. Nev., 2005)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, in part, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Williams v. Bodo, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 43032 (D. Nev., June 11, 2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Remanded by Williams v. State, 2013 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 1605 (2013)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Williams v. State, 2016 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 483 (2016)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Clark County District Court (Nevada) of six counts of driving with a prohibited substance in the blood or urine, one count of use of a controlled substance, and one count of possession of a controlled substance. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 484.379(3) violated the Equal Protection Clause because it impermissibly treated drivers with proscribed levels of drugs in their systems differently from others. The supreme court disagreed. Traffic safety was a rational basis for upholding statutes that regulated the use of substances that might impair a person's ability to drive. Banning driving by persons with any measurable amount of illicit drugs was constitutional because the legislature was reasonable in determining that there was no level of illicit drug use which could be acceptably combined with driving a vehicle; the established potential for lethal consequences was too great. The governmental interest in maintaining safe highways was sufficient for the prohibited substance statute to survive a constitutional attack on the basis that it impermissibly treated drivers with the proscribed levels of illicit drugs in their system differently from others.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Chavez v. Sievers


No. 34580, No. 34932


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 288; 43 P.3d 1022; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 39; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 28



April 12, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied May 2, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Consolidated appeals from an order granting respondents' motion for summary judgment and from an order granting respondents' motion for attorney fees. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Steven P. Elliott, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employee filed a complaint against defendant individuals and defendant former employer asserting that he was wrongfully terminated from his job on the basis of his race. The Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County, Nevada, rendered summary judgment in favor of the individuals and the employer, and granted them attorneys fees under the provisions of Nev. R. Civ. P. 68 and Nev. Rev. Stat. § 17.115. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee's claims included assault, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and tortious discharge. The employee amended the complaint to include a claim for violation of his civil rights under Title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e et seq., and under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1985(3). The employer had less than 15 employees. The Supreme Court of Nevada found that: (1) it declined to recognize a public policy exception to the employment at-will doctrine based on race discrimination with respect to small employers because the legislature had created remedies for employment discrimination and had explicitly exempted small employers; (2) the district court did not err in granting the employers' motion for summary judgment as to the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1985(3) cause of action because § 1985(3) created no rights, as it was only a remedial statute; and (3) the district court did not abuse its discretion in awarding attorney fees because both Nev. R. Civ. P. 68 and Nev. Rev. Stat. § 17.115 provided for multiple parties making a joint offer of judgment and the district court weighed the Beattie factors.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parent filed a petition for a writ of mandamus, seeking to compel the Nevada District Court to vacate an order issuing a marriage license allowing petitioner's 15-year-old daughter to marry, and he sought to annul the marriage.


OVERVIEW: In this case, the court was asked to determine the constitutionality of Nev. Rev.
