553 of 673 DOCUMENTS


State v. Kopp


No. 36457


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 199; 43 P.3d 340; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 32; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 22



April 5, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order dismissing two misdemeanor counts against respondent. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Sally L. Loehrer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a decision of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, which ruled that it lacked jurisdiction over certain misdemeanor charges that were joined in a single indictment or information charging defendant with a felony or gross misdemeanor and dismissed the misdemeanor charges.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the district court acquired jurisdiction pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 173.115 over misdemeanors that were joined with a felony or gross misdemeanor in a single indictment or information. Defendant argued that § 173.115 was most reasonably interpreted as only permitting the joinder of felonies and those misdemeanors denominated as gross misdemeanors. The instant court concluded that the district court did not acquire jurisdiction over misdemeanors that had been joined in a single indictment or information with a felony or gross misdemeanor. It expressly rejected the State's contention that § 173.115 should be interpreted as granting jurisdiction to district courts over a misdemeanor that was joined with a felony or gross misdemeanor in a single indictment or information. It was more reasonable to interpret § 173.115 as not granting district courts jurisdiction over misdemeanors under these circumstances because it avoided the unreasonable result of having an overburdened district court system, as well as an underutilized justices' court system.


OUTCOME: The district court's decision was affirmed.

Abrego v. State


No. 36425


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 54; 38 P.3d 868; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 4; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 6



January 24, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction pursuant to a jury verdict of three counts of sale of a controlled substance within 1,000 feet of a school and one count of possession of a controlled substance. Sixth Judicial District Court, Humboldt County; Richard Wagner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Sixth Judicial District Court, Humboldt County (Nevada) convicted defendant of three counts of sale of a controlled substance and one count of simple possession. Thereafter, defendant was sentenced to 12 to 48 months in prison for each sales count, with an enhancement for being within 1,000 feet of a school. In addition, defendant was sentenced to a concurrent term of imprisonment of 12 to 30 months for possession. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that the district court committed reversible constitutional error by determining the sentence enhancement, rather than submitting the issue to the jury. Defendant argued that Apprendi required that any fact, other than a prior conviction, that increased the penalty for a crime beyond the prescribed statutory maximum was required to be submitted to the jury and proven beyond a reasonable doubt. In addition, defendant argued that the application of the sentence enhancement was not harmless because such an argument ignored the central importance of the Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial. Defendant's failure to present any evidence to dispute that his residence was not within 1,000 feet of the schoolyard together with his specific request that the district court, not the jury, determine the distance of his residence from the schoolyard constituted a waiver of his rights under Apprendi. While an Apprendi violation occurred in defendant's trial and sentencing, defendant affirmatively waived his protections under Apprendi and could not complain of the violation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Rhyne v. State


No. 36064


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


118 Nev. 1; 38 P.3d 163; 2002 Nev. LEXIS 1; 118 Nev. Adv. Rep. 1



January 16, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Rhyne v. State, 121 Nev. 1165, 152 P.3d 801, 2005 Nev. LEXIS 1361 (2005)

Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by State v. Rhyne, 2014 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 157 (2014)

OVERVIEW: The defendant was at a bar when he saw an individual that he hated. The evidence at trial indicated that the defendant killed the victim and then had his friend help him put the victim in a dumpster. During the trial, defense counsel did not want to call a certain witness fearing it would do more harm then good. The court considered and dismissed the defendant's other challenges.

556 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Salaiscooper v. Eighth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 38296


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 892; 34 P.3d 509; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 79; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 72



November 15, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The petitioner, a prostitute, contended that the district attorney's prosecution policy violated the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution. On appeal from the Las Vegas Justice Court, the Clark County District Court (Nevada) concluded the policy was constitutional. Thereafter the district court stayed the justice court proceedings until the instant court addressed the constitutional issues by way of extraordinary writ.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney's office implemented a policy that prohibited plea negotiations with sellers of sex who were charged with solicitation of prostitution. The prostitute contended the policy discriminated against females and sought one of three extraordinary writs to obtain relief from alleged selective prosecution: a writ of certiorari, a writ of mandamus, or a writ of prohibition. The supreme court noted it was constitutionally permissible to treat prostitutes differently than their customers. The supreme court held the policy did not violate the Equal Protection Clauses of the United States and Nevada Constitutions, and concluded that extraordinary relief was not warranted. Moreover, the legislature had vested the district attorney with prosecutorial discretion and it was within the purview of his prosecution powers to treat buyers of sex differently than sellers of sex. Finally, the supreme court held the prostitute failed to prove the district attorney's decision to prosecute arose from an impermissible desire to discriminate on the basis of race, gender, or other protected class. Therefore, neither the federal or state constitutions compelled intervention.


OUTCOME: Because the case was one of prosecutorial discretion and not unconstitutional selective prosecution, the prostitute's petition for extraordinary relief was denied.

 

557 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Pellegrini v. State


No. 35999


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 860; 34 P.3d 519; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 80; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 71



November 15, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court denying appellant's post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus in a death penalty case. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Kathy A. Hardcastle, Judge.
Pellegrini v. State, 104 Nev. 625, 764 P.2d 484, 1988 Nev. LEXIS 98 (1988)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant petitioner appealed an order of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, which denied his postconviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus in a death penalty case.


OVERVIEW: The issue in this case was whether Nev. Rev. Stat. § 34.726 and its one-year time bar applied to second or successive petitions for postconviction relief. The petitioner argued that § 34.726 applied just to first petitions and that dismissal for delayed filing of second or successive petitions was governed only by the laches provisions of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 34.800. The instant court rejected this contention and concluded that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 34.726 applied to all postconviction petitions, including successive petitions. The doctrine of equitable estoppel did not prevent the State from asserting and the courts from applying § 34.726 in cases of successive petitions. The instant court found that § 34.726 was not constitutionally infirm, but was a reasonable regulation of the right to pursue habeas corpus. The petitioner failed to allege a sufficient basis to show good cause to excuse the procedural bars or to conclude that a fundamental miscarriage of justice would have occurred from the failure to consider his claims for relief on the merits. Thus, the trial court did not err in declining to conduct an evidentiary hearing on these issues.


OUTCOME: The instant court affirmed the trial court's order denying the petitioner's untimely and successive petition for a writ of habeas corpus.

Servin v. State


No.35884


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 775; 32 P.3d 1277; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 69; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 65



October 17, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Servin v. State, 120 Nev. 1283, 131 P.3d 638, 2004 Nev. LEXIS 1210 (2004)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Servin v. McDaniels, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 119618 (D. Nev., Mar. 27, 2008)
Related proceeding at Servin v. Williams, 2013 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 1516 (2013)
Related proceeding at Servin v. Williams, 2014 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 114 (2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] This is an appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of first degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, and from a sentence of death. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Brent T. Adams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, vacated in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada) of first degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and robbery with the use of a deadly weapon. He was sentenced to death. Defendant challenged the conviction and sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and two co-defendants murdered and robbed a woman in her home. Defendant was 16 years old at the time. The trial court did not err in relying on reasonable doubt instructions based on Nev. Rev. Stat. § 175.211. The death penalty statutory scheme performed its narrowing function. Sentencing defendant to death did not violate the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights. The aggravating circumstances of burglary and home invasion were duplicative. Sufficient evidence supported the aggravating circumstance of torture under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 200.033(8). The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for a new trial based upon newly discovered evidence. However, the imposition of the death penalty was excessive. The quality of the evidence against defendant as the shooter was problematic, and his youth had significant value as a mitigating factor pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 200.035(6).


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed, but the death sentence was vacated and the case was remanded for imposition of two consecutive terms of life in prison without the possibility of parole.

 

559 of 673 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Harvey v. Second Judicial Dist. Ct


No. 35144


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 754; 32 P.3d 1263; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 68; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 64



October 10, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This was an original proceeding by complaint and information in the nature of quo warranto. The complaint arose from a disagreement between petitioner, the Washoe County Clerk, and respondent, the Second Judicial District Court (Nevada), over the district court's assumption of the supervision and control of the court clerk's position.


OVERVIEW: The county clerk argued that the county clerk was the sole person constitutionally designated as responsible for performing the duties associated with the court clerk. According to the county clerk, the district court had usurped her position as ex officio court clerk of the district court in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 35.120. The instant court held that the district court did not usurp the office of county clerk by assuming direct control over the functions of the court clerk. The court clerk was not a constitutional office. Rather, it was a ministerial office inherent to the judicial branch of government. The court clerk's duties could be performed, in whole or in part, either by the county clerk pursuant to legislative enactment, or by the district court pursuant to court rule. The county clerk had no inherent, inalienable, or unbridled right to perform the office of court clerk without direction from the district court. Thus, the district court had the authority to supervise the county clerk when the county clerk was acting in the capacity of court clerk.


OUTCOME: The action for quo warranto was dismissed.

Washington v. State


No. 36589


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 735; 30 P.3d 1134; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 63; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 62



September 17, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended December 3, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a guilty plea, of the felony of sale of an imitation controlled substance, representing it to be a controlled substance. Second Judicial District Court, Jerome M. Polaha and James W. Hardesty, Judges. 1


1  This case was initially assigned to District Judge Jerome M. Polaha who heard and denied appellant's motions to strike and to dismiss based on a conflict between NRS 453.323 and NRS 453.332. The case was subsequently re-assigned to Judge James W. Hardesty, on a calendar overflow basis, who accepted appellant's change of plea to guilty pursuant to negotiations under which appellant reserved the right to appeal Judge Polaha's ruling on the conflict between NRS 453.323 and NRS 453.332.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with felony sale of an imitation controlled substance, representing it to be a controlled substance, in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.323, and he filed a motion to dismiss the charge. The Second Judicial District Court (Nevada) denied defendant's motion, and convicted him of the charge after he entered a conditional plea of guilty. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with felony sale of an imitation controlled substance, representing it to be a controlled substance, after he sold a substance to an undercover police officer which was supposed to be, but was not, cocaine. At trial, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charge, arguing that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.323 was repealed by Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.332 because § 453.332 prohibited the same conduct but punished it as a misdemeanor. The state supreme court held that Nev. Rev. Stat. §§ 453.323 and 453.332 prohibited the same conduct, and because Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.332 was enacted after Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.323, § 453.323 was repealed by implication.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court vacated defendant's conviction and sentence based on Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.323, and remanded the case for proceedings under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 453.332.

Barton v. State


No. 33143


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 686; 30 P.3d 1103; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 59; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 56



September 12, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Barton v. State, 122 Nev. 1649, 178 P.3d 733, 2006 Nev. LEXIS 283 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Lee A. Gates, Judge.
Barton v. State, 112 Nev. 1705, 999 P.2d 365, 1996 Nev. LEXIS 1448 (1996)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant was convicted of second-degree murder in the Clark County District Court (Nevada). The supreme court dismissed the defendant's direct appeal. He then filed a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus which the trial court denied. The defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The defendant was involved in a confrontation that ended in him ramming the victim's car and killing him. The defendant argued that the trial court erred in dismissing his writ of habeas corpus because: (1) his trial counsel was ineffective for failing to request a jury instruction on the lesser charge of reckless driving causing substantial bodily harm; and (2) his appellate counsel was ineffective for failing to argue that Nev. Rev. Stat. § 200.070 was unconstitutionally vague. The supreme court held that the trial court had not erred because: (1) the proper test for whether an offense was a lesser included offense was whether the elements of a lesser offense were an entirely included subset of the elements of the charged offense (the supreme court arrived at that conclusion by overruling Owens v. State, 100 Nev. 286, 680 P.2d 593 (1984)); and (2) § 200.070 simply distinguished manslaughter from murder by referring to the factors which indicated malice, and none of the minor traffic violations cited by the defendant would naturally tend to destroy the life of a human being, thus, the commission of those minor offenses would not automatically render a killing murder.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Villanueva v. State


No. 36348


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 664; 27 P.3d 443; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 52; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 53



July 25, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Villanueva v. State, 2015 Nev. App. Unpub. LEXIS 108 (2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a guilty plea, of attempted murder with the use of a deadly weapon on school property. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph T. Bonaventure, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada, which sentenced him to life imprisonment with the possibility of parole after 20 years under Nev. Rev. Stat. 193.161(2), the on-school-property sentencing alternative, after it convicted him of attempted murder with the use of a deadly weapon on school property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a gang member, shot and injured two high school students who were members of a rival gang, on the grounds of a high school. Defendant challenged his sentence contending that Nev. Rev. Stat. 193.161(2) was (1) void for vagueness; (2) an unconstitutional delegation of the legislature's duty to define crimes and affix punishments; or (3) inconsistent with Nev. Rev. Stat. 193.330. The Nevada Supreme Court found that: (1) the sentencing provisions of Nev. Rev. Stat. 193.161(2), the on-school-property alternative, were not unconstitutionally vague because they clearly set forth the consequences of the crimes defined; (2) the sentencing scheme was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority because the legislature could grant the courts discretion to sentence according to a range of sentences; and (3) the district court was not required to sentence defendant under the attempt statute, Nev. Rev. Stat. 193.330, because the on-school-property alternative prescribed a different penalty.


OUTCOME: The district court's sentence was affirmed.

 

563 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Finger v. State


No. 32716


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 548; 27 P.3d 66; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 50; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 48



July 24, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected August 2, 2001. Certiorari Denied February 19, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 647.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of conviction, pursuant to a plea of guilty but mentally ill, of second-degree murder and a sentence of life imprisonment. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph S. Pavlikowski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was charged with one count of open murder. The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) denied his request to enter a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity, and appellant subsequently entered a plea of "guilty but mentally ill" to a charge of second-degree murder. The court convicted him on that charge and sentenced him to life imprisonment. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant alleged that Nevada's abolishment of insanity as an affirmative defense violated the Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments to the United States Constitution, and Nev. Const. art. 1, §§ 6 and 8(5). The Supreme Court of Nevada held that the current statutory scheme would permit an individual to be convicted of a criminal offense under circumstances where the individual lacked the mental capacity to form the applicable intent to commit the crime, a necessary element of the offense. The legislative history involving Senate Bill 314 (Nev. 1995) and the amendment of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 193.220 led appellant to believe he would have been prohibited from arguing legal insanity as defined by M'Naghten; consequently his plea of "guilty, but mentally ill" was not knowingly entered. The court concluded that the statutory scheme violated the due process clauses of the United States and Nevada Constitutions. In light of that conclusion, it was unnecessary to address appellant's arguments regarding cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of conviction and remanded with instructions to permit appellant to withdraw his plea of guilty but mentally ill, and enter a plea of not guilty in accordance with the court's opinion.

United States v. State Eng'r


No. 32740


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 585; 27 P.3d 51; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 53; 32 ELR 20028; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 49



July 24, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court denying a petition for judicial review. Ninth Judicial District Court, Douglas County; David R. Gamble, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Ninth Judicial District Court, Douglas County, Nevada, denied petitioner's, United States Department of the Interior, Bureau of Land Management (BLM), petition for judicial review of respondent's, State Engineer, State of Nevada, ruling on stockwater permits on public lands. The BLM appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State denied nine applications filed by the BLM for stockwater permits, finding that the BLM was not a qualified applicant for stockwater permits. The BLM contended that the plain language of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 533.503 authorized the State to issue stockwater permits to the BLM, and if it did not, the statute was unconstitutional. The BLM argued that the phrase "legally entitled" was not ambiguous and meant that either the landowner, or a person with the landowner's permission to use the land, was legally entitled to place livestock on the land. The statute was not ambiguous. To say that the United States, as owner of the public land, had to issue itself a permit to lease or graze livestock upon the land was an illogical and unreasonable construction of the statutory language. The statute indicated it required an applicant to have some legal authority to use the public land for grazing purposes and nothing else. The BLM was a qualified applicant under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 533.503.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Evans v. State


No. 35641


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 609; 28 P.3d 498; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 54; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 50



July 24, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order denying a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus in a capital case. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Jeffrey D. Sobel, Judge.
Evans v. State, 112 Nev. 1172, 926 P.2d 265, 1996 Nev. LEXIS 152 (Oct. 22, 1996)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death. The Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), denied his post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus without holding an evidentiary hearing. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: A jury returned death penalty verdicts on four counts of first-degree murder. Defendant claimed his trial and appellate counsel were ineffective in numerous ways. Several of defendant's arguments were rejected flat out, and others were rejected because defendant failed to establish prejudice. However, defendant's claim that his attorneys were ineffective for failing to object to the prosecutor's comments during the penalty phase of the trial had merit. Some of the prosecutor's remarks were excessive and unacceptable and should have been challenged. The prosecutor's words were particularly designed to stir the jury's passion and appeal to partiality. The prosecutor incorrectly informed the jurors that they did not have to wait until a certain point in the deliberation to consider other matter evidence. The instructions did not cure the error introduced by the incorrect argument. A new penalty hearing was therefore required.


OUTCOME: The district court's order upholding defendant's conviction was affirmed. However, the death sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded for a new penalty hearing.

In re Discipline of Schaefer


No. 36173


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 496; 25 P.3d 191; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 44; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 44



June 21, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 10, 2001. Certiorari Denied February 19, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 704.


PRIOR HISTORY: Automatic appeal from a decision by a Southern Nevada Disciplinary Board hearing panel, recommending disbarment.



DISPOSITION: Recommendation approved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A hearing panel of the Southern Nevada Disciplinary Board recommended disbarring respondent attorney after finding that he had violated several professional conduct rules. The attorney claimed he did not violate Nev. Sup. Ct. R. 182 by contacting represented parties while he was representing himself.


OVERVIEW: Disciplinary complaints were brought against the attorney. The hearing panel found that he violated several professional conduct rules, including Rule 182, which prohibited him from directly contacting a represented party without the consent of that party's counsel. Rule 182 applied to communications by a lawyer appearing pro se or on behalf of his corporation. However, the rule as applied to the attorney when he was representing himself was unconstitutionally vague. The attorney could have reasonably concluded that the rule did not apply in situations where he was self-represented. The attorney's request for a continuance was properly denied. He had four weeks to obtain new counsel and to prepare for the hearing. By offering to dismiss a party from a conspiracy case in exchange for favorable testimony in a criminal case, the attorney violated Nev. Sup. Ct. R. 173(6). A pattern of misconduct was shown, and the attorney failed to acknowledge that his conduct was wrongful.


OUTCOME: The recommendation that the attorney be disbarred was approved. Violations based on instances in which the attorney was representing himself could not be considered in determining the appropriate discipline. However, the bulk of the violations were supported by clear and convincing evidence, and disbarment was warranted.

Gallego v. State


No. 35291


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 348; 23 P.3d 227; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 33; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 33



May 17, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] This is an appeal from a judgment of conviction and sentence of death following a second penalty hearing. Sixth Judicial District Court, Pershing County; John S. McGroarty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two murders and sentenced to death. A federal court ordered that defendant be resentenced. Defendant received a new penalty hearing in the Sixth Judicial District Court, Pershing County, Nevada, and was again sentenced to death. Defendant appealed from the judgments of conviction and death sentences following the second penalty hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that numerous errors occurred at his second penalty hearing, including that the district court erred in not permitting him to represent himself and in not appointing substitute counsel. The record showed that defendant repeatedly and intentionally obstructed the district court proceedings, which supported the district court's finding that he was unable or unwilling to abide by rules of procedure and courtroom protocol. The Nevada Supreme Court found that: (1) the evidence supported the aggravating circumstances, (2) there was no indication that the death sentence was imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any arbitrary factor, (3) considering the crime, the sentence was not excessive, (4) the district court did not err in denying defendant's motion to represent himself or his motion for substitute counsel, and (5) defendant's other assignments of error did not warrant relief.


OUTCOME: The judgments of conviction and death sentences were affirmed.

Hudson v. Ignacio


No. 34666


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 387; 22 P.3d 1154; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 35; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 35



May 17, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court denying appellant's post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Sixth Judicial District Court, Pershing County; Richard Wagner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to and was convicted of driving under the influence of a controlled substance causing substantial bodily harm, and unlawful possession of a controlled substance. After the dismissal of his direct appeal, defendant filed a post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The Sixth Judicial District Court, Pershing County, Nevada, denied the petition. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: There was a discrepancy in the plea agreement with respect to the maximum sentence that defendant could receive. The district court relied on the prior convictions shown in the presentence investigation report (PSI) to determine defendant's sentencing enhancements without hearing evidence of prior convictions. The Nevada Supreme Court found that: (1) defendant's appellate counsel was ineffective for failing to raise the issue of the validity of the prior conviction enhancements on appeal, (2) to enhance a conviction under Nev. Rev. Stat. 453.336, the State had to produce records of the prior convictions that contain prima facie evidence of the prior convictions unless the defendant stipulates to or waives the requirement of proof, (3) the district court erred in relying on the PSI to enhance defendant's conviction, (4) the location of the driving under the influence offense was within the subject matter jurisdiction of the district court, and (5) defendant's pleas were not knowingly made because he did not understand the consequences of the pleas.


OUTCOME: The district court's denial of the post-conviction petition for a writ of habeas corpus was reversed, and the matter was remanded with instructions to permit defendant to withdraw his pleas, and for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

 

569 of 673 DOCUMENTS


S.O.C., Inc. v. Mirage Casino-Hotel


No. 34563


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 403; 23 P.3d 243; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 37; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 36



May 17, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order issuing a preliminary injunction enjoining the distribution of commercial handbills on private property along the Las Vegas Strip. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Michael A. Cherry, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants contested a decision of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), granting respondents' request for a preliminary injunction in respondents' action alleging that appellants' practice of hiring canvassers to pass out leaflets on respondents' sidewalks soliciting business for erotic dance services constituted a trespass.


OVERVIEW: Respondents were owners of private property who had conveyed to the relevant county a perpetual pedestrian easement across certain sidewalks that were owned by respondents. Respondents then sued appellant providers of referrals for erotic dance entertainment, alleging that appellants' practice of hiring canvassers to pass out leaflets on the sidewalks soliciting business for erotic dance services constituted a trespass. The trial court granted respondents' request for a preliminary injunction and appellants challenged that determination. The supreme court affirmed. The easement, by its express language, was limited to pedestrian use of the sidewalks for travel purposes. Accordingly, the existence of the easement alone did not transform the private sidewalks into a public forum for purposes of U.S. Const. amend. I. Furthermore, the trial court did not err in making a preliminary finding that the sidewalks were private property and therefore not subject to the reach of amend. I. Also, an injunction was an appropriate remedy for the threat of continuing trespass.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision granting respondents' request for a preliminary injunction was affirmed. Despite the existence of an easement permitting travel by pedestrians, respondents' privately owned sidewalks constituted private property that was not subject to the reach of constitutional free speech guarantees.

Banegas v. State Indus. Ins. Sys.


No. 29833


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 222; 19 P.3d 245; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 23; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 22



March 16, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court denying a petition for judicial review. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph S. Pavlikowski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant's claim for death benefits after the death of her ex-husband, whom she had reconciled with but did not remarry, was denied by the State Industrial Insurance System, and this denial was affirmed by the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County, Nevada. She sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant divorced the decedent after 26 years of marriage; they reconciled, but did not remarry, and at the time of decedent's death, he was paying all of appellant's non-housing expenses. She sought death benefits from the SIIS, since the decedent died from a work-related disease. The SIIS denied her claim, on grounds that she was not an eligible dependent under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 616C.505(8). She appealed to the lower court, which affirmed. She sought further review; the appellate court affirmed. The appellate court found that § 616C.505(8) was ambiguous, and resorted to rules of statutory construction to interpret it. In doing so, it noted that the Nevada legislature, by adopting Nev. Rev. Stat. § 616A.010, abolished the common law rule that the workers' compensation statutes were to be construed liberally in favor of an injured employee or his dependents. It held that § 616C.505(8) provided death benefits for those who had a legally recognized relationship to, and were partially dependent upon, the deceased employee. As appellant had no such legally recognized relationship with decedent, the SIIS properly denied her claim for benefits.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed, because as ex-spouse of decedent, appellant had no legally recognized relationship with him, and thus was not a dependent eligible for death benefits as defined by the workers' compensation statute.

 

571 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Rogers v. Heller


No. 37189


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 169; 18 P.3d 1034; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 17; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 17



February 27, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the district court denying appellants' complaint, which challenged the legal sufficiency of an initiative petition. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; Michael R. Griffin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants filed a complaint pursuant to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 295.061, challenging the legal sufficiency of respondent's initiative petition. The First Judicial District Court, Carson City (Nevada) denied the claim, and appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Respondent state education association filed an initiative for submission to the legislature, seeking to increase funding for the state's public schools. Appellants, numerous businesses, chambers of commerce, and business associations, challenged the initiative. The district court denied the challenge. On appeal, the supreme court reversed, holding that the initiative failed to comply with Nev. Const. art. 19, § 6, which precluded any financial appropriation or expenditure without also imposing a tax sufficient to fund it. Although the legislature had a perpetual duty to fund education, it was not required to continue such funding at any particular level. A necessary appropriation or expenditure in any set amount or percentage was a new requirement that otherwise did not exist, and the proposed additional 4 percent tax was clearly insufficient to cover the initiative's required 50-percent-of-revenue appropriation. Further, the offending provision was not severable from the rest of the proposal.


OUTCOME: Trial court judgment reversed. The initiative required a new appropriation and expenditure, and the proposed additional tax was not sufficient to cover the appropriation, as the state constitution required. Second, the initiative was to be kept substantively intact, so the offending provision could not be severed. Therefore the legislature was barred from taking further action on the proposal.

 

572 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Mangarella v. State


No. 35212


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 130; 17 P.3d 989; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 11; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 14



February 16, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to an Alford plea, of one count of lewdness with a minor. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Michael L. Douglas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction for a sex-related offense, challenging the constitutionality of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 176A.410.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued (1) the polygraph requirement of § 176A.410 was overbroad, thus infringing on his privilege against self-incrimination, and (2) the statute was unconstitutionally vague because it did not define residence, work, and curfew requirements. The court affirmed the conviction, finding the statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad or vague. As to overbreadth, the statute did not permit a probation officer to conduct a polygraph examination on any issue, except those relating to use of controlled substances by a probationer. Further the statute allowed a valid assertion of the privilege against self-incrimination if the probationer were asked for incriminating statements regarding crimes for which he had not been convicted. As to vagueness, because a person of common intelligence need not guess as to the statute's meaning and it was required to be read to further special needs inherent in supervising sex offenders, the statute was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction was affirmed. The statute was neither unconstitutionally overbroad or vague.

 

573 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Reinkemeyer v. Safeco Ins. Co. of Am.


No. 34507


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 44; 16 P.3d 1069; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 6; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 5



January 30, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certified questions of law pursuant to NRAP 5 from the United States District Court concerning the applicability of NRS 687B.385 and the constitutionality of that statute. United States District Court, District of Nevada; Howard D. McKibben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Questions answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court, District of Nevada, certified two questions to the Supreme Court (Nevada) pursuant to Nev. R. App. P. 5. The first was whether former Nev. Rev. Stat. § 687B.385, which prohibited cancellation or nonrenewal of certain insurance policies, applied to homeowner's policies. The second was if the former Nev. Rev. Stat. § 687B.385 as applied to homeowner's policies, violated the United States and Nevada Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insured sued respondent insurer for failing to renew the appellant's homeowner's policy, claiming the respondent violated former Nev. Rev. Stat. § 687B.385, and the federal court certified two questions to the court. Respondent argued that the statute only applied to automobile insurance, not homeowner's policies. Respondent also argued that the statute was unconstitutional because it did not guarantee a fair and adequate return. The court held that the statute did apply to homeowner's policies, and that the statute was not facially unconstitutional under the Nevada Constitution. By its terms, the statute applied to homeowner's insurance policies, and the legislative history of the 1997 amendment did not change the construction of the earlier version. Additionally, the constitutional requirement of a fair and reasonable rate of return did not apply to individual policies. Instead, the constitutional rule applied to the entire range of policies issued by an insurer. While the statute could necessitate respondent sustaining a loss on an individual policy, it did not deprive respondent of a fair and reasonable rate of return generally on homeowner's protection.


OUTCOME: The former version of the statute did apply to homeowner's policies. Although the court did not have the authority to answer the second question as to the constitutionality of the statute under the United States Constitution, the court determined that the Nevada Constitution required that price fixing regulations guarantee a fair and reasonable return, and the statute was not facially invalid under that standard.

 

574 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Leonard v. State


No. 33732


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


117 Nev. 53; 17 P.3d 397; 2001 Nev. LEXIS 5; 117 Nev. Adv. Rep. 6

Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Leonard v. McDaniel, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 18650 (D. Nev., Mar. 31, 2006)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Leonard v. State, 2017 Nev. Unpub. LEXIS 934 (Oct. 23, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count each of first degree murder and robbery, and from a sentence of death. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Michael L. Douglas, Judge.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), convicting him of first degree murder and robbery, and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in trial court of first degree murder and robbery and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, claiming the trial court erred during jury selection, the State failed to preserve evidence, the trial court improperly limited his cross-examination of a witness, the trial court failed to order disclosure of presentence investigation reports, the State's actions during its witness examination were prejudicial, the evidence was insufficient, the jury instructions were insufficient, there was prosecutorial misconduct, and the death penalty was unconstitutional. 575 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Blackjack Bonding v. City of Las Vegas Mun. Court


No. 33345


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 1213; 14 P.3d 1275; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 141; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 128



December 29, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order dismissing a complaint to recover filing fees paid by Las Vegas bail bond sureties to the Las Vegas Municipal Court. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Kathy A. Hardcastle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bail bond sureties requested review of an order of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) dismissing their complaint against appellees city municipal court and city to recover bail bond filing fees paid by them to appellee city municipal court.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bail bond sureties commenced a suit to recover bail bond filing fees paid by them to appellee city municipal court. The other appellee party was the municipality. The district court ordered the suit dismissed. The court, in the case of first impression, reviewed Nev. Const. art. 3, § 1; art. 6, §§ 1, 9, and several statutory sources of judicial powers to conclude that a Nevada municipal court possessed the authority to collect reasonable fees pursuant to its inherent judicial powers. Those fees included reasonable filing fees on bail bonds. That inherent authority existed prior to and after the May 12, 1997 effective date of the more explicit authority of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 5.073(2) that provided that municipal courts could charge and collect fees prescribed in Nev. Rev. Stat. § 4.060(1)(o): that each justice of the peace could charge and collect $ 40 for each bail or property bond filed. By granting municipal courts general jurisdictional power over bail and property bonds, the legislature also granted by implication every power necessary for municipal courts to exercise that power, including the power to charge and collect reasonable filing fees.


OUTCOME: The order of dismissal was affirmed. The Nevada Constitution and various provisions of Nevada statutes indicated that Nevada municipal courts had inherent power to charge and collect reasonable fees. Therefore, appellee municipal court legitimately assessed filing fees on bail bonds.

 

576 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Wegner v. State


No. 33928


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 1149; 14 P.3d 25; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 131; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 120



December 5, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of first degree murder. The court sentenced appellant to life in prison with the possibility of parole after twenty years. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Mark W. Gibbons, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged her first degree murder conviction from the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), contending the trial court erred by allowing flawed jury instructions and omitting certain of them.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder. On appeal, she contended the trial court erred in charging the jury with a flawed instruction on malice aforethought and in denying her instruction on involuntary manslaughter. The court agreed, reversing her conviction, reasoning that the jury instruction on malice aforethought created an unconstitutional mandatory presumption of malice aforethought, which incorrectly relieved the State of its burden to prove every element of the crime charged. Further, use of the instruction was not harmless error, due to the fact that the case lacked an overwhelming evidence of guilt. It was thus not clear beyond a reasonable doubt that the jury would have found appellant guilty of first degree murder absent the erroneous jury instruction. The court further erred in denying appellant's request for an involuntary manslaughter instruction because evidence revealed that the victim's death could have occurred by a fall or some other means besides child abuse, as the State alleged. In such a circumstance, the death would not have been a product of malice aforethought but possibly a result of neglect or endangerment.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded. The improper jury instruction on malice aforethought and the omission of the involuntary manslaughter jury instruction mandated reversal of appellant's conviction. Where guilt was determined by an evaluation of directly contradictory expert testimony, use of such evidence deprived appellant of her right to due process.

Citizens for Honest & Responsible Gov't v. Heller


No. 34488


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 939; 11 P.3d 121; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 114; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 102



October 26, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order denying judicial review and affirming the Secretary of State's decision that appellant's recall petition failed. First Judicial District Court, Carson City; Michael E. Fondi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a group seeking to recall a county commissioner, claimed the First Judicial District Court, Carson City (Nevada) erred in affirming respondent secretary of state's finding that a recall petition failed. The district court concluded that recall statutes Nev. Rev. Code §§ 306.015(5), .040(1) were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, a citizens group seeking to recall respondent county commissioner, filed recall petitions, which respondent secretary of state determined were unsuccessful. The 60-day limitation in Nev. Rev. Code § 306.015(3) did not violate petitioners' U.S. Const. amend. I rights. State's important regulatory interests were sufficient to justify the restrictions. Sixty-day limitation did not impose a severe burden on petitioners. Recall statutes promoted the efficient regulation of recall petitions. Recall procedures and statistical sampling did not fail to aid the operation of the recall right. Petitioners presented no evidence showing that the statistical procedure utilized was inaccurate. Nevada Administrative Procedure Act (APA), Nev. Rev. Code § 233B, did not apply to hearing, but use of APA was harmless because petitioners were still permitted to advance additional evidence under traditional evidence rules.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. Recall provisions did not violate constitutional rights. Nevada Administrative Procedure Act did not apply to review proceedings, but reliance on it was harmless. Statutes promoted efficient regulation of recall petitions. Important regulatory interests sufficiently outweighed burden.

Paschall v. State


No. 34288


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 911; 8 P.3d 851; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 109; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 99



September 19, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a bench trial, of two counts of felony driving under the influence. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Jerome Polaha, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada) convicting him of two counts of felony driving under the influence of alcohol.


OVERVIEW: After a bench trial, defendant was convicted of two counts of driving under the influence of liquor. Defendant's offenses were enhanced to felonies under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 484.3792(1)(c) because he had twice been convicted of misdemeanor driving under the influence. On appeal, defendant argued his prior offenses were improperly used to enhance his subsequent offenses. The court held defendant's misdemeanor convictions were valid because any failure to properly sentence him did not render such convictions void. Regardless, pursuant to Nev. Const. art. 6, § 8, the legislature had authority to statutorily grant the justice courts jurisdiction to suspend sentences. The justice courts thus properly suspended defendant's misdemeanor sentences. The court concluded the county ordinance under which defendant was previously convicted did not require approval of the board of directors of the Nevada Department of Transportation because it was not enacted under Nev. Rev. Stat. § 484.779. The court affirmed defendant's convictions because the prior misdemeanor convictions were properly considered for enhancement purposes.


OUTCOME: Order convicting defendant of two counts of felony driving under the influence of alcohol was affirmed because defendant's misdemeanor convictions were properly used to enhance defendant's subsequent offenses; the justice courts had jurisdiction to suspend defendant's misdemeanor sentences and one county ordinance under which defendant was sentenced did not require administrative approval.

Hollaway v. State


No. 32142


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 732; 6 P.3d 987; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 95; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 83



August 23, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count of first-degree murder and a sentence of death. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Jeffrey D. Sobel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Conviction affirmed; sentence reversed; remanded for new penalty hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of conviction for first-degree murder and death sentence that were imposed by the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada). On appeal, the court also conducted a mandatory review of the death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for first-degree murder and subsequent death sentence. The court concluded that the grounds raised by defendant on appeal were meritless. However, pursuant to the court's mandatory review of the death sentence, the court concluded that defendant's sentence was imposed under the influence of prejudicial and arbitrary factors. The conviction was affirmed, the death sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded for a new penalty hearing. The court concluded that the unprovoked electric shocking of defendant at his penalty hearing and in the presence of the jurors was an incident constituting an arbitrary and prejudicial factor requiring reversal of the sentence. The court also concluded that the fact that the jury was not informed of their responsibility to independently assess all the evidence in considering mitigation by the defendant, including that they were allowed to consider any evidence presented in the guilt phase that could have indicated that a penalty less than death was appropriate, was an additional arbitrary and prejudicial factor requiring reversal of the death sentence.


OUTCOME: Conviction was affirmed, death sentence was vacated, and case was remanded for a new penalty hearing. In connection with death sentence, court concluded that jury was improperly influenced by a prejudicial and arbitrary factor, as defendant had experienced an unprovoked electric shocking at his penalty hearing in the jurors' presence.

Evans v. Dean Witter Reynolds, Inc.


No. 30843


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 598; 5 P.3d 1043; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 86; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 71



August 18, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal and cross-appeal from a judgment of the district court, pursuant to a jury verdict, in an action for conspiracy to convert personal and real property. Ninth Judicial District Court, Douglas County; Michael R. Griffin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant and respondent appealed and cross-appealed from the judgment of the Ninth Judicial District Court, Douglas County (Nevada), which granted judgment upon a favorable jury verdict against respondent on appellant's charges of conspiracy to commit conversion of securities.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sued respondents, an investment firm and its senior vice president, for conspiracy to commit conversion of securities. A jury awarded significant compensatory damages, jointly and severally, against respondents, and rendered separate punitive damage awards against respondents. The district court reduced the compensatory damage award to zero by applying equitable offsets, but let the punitive damage awards stand. On appeal, appellant argued the equitable offsets were improper; respondent argued that punitive damages were not warranted. The supreme court affirmed in part, holding that respondent acted maliciously or with a conscious disregard of appellant's interests, warranting punitive damages. The court reversed and remanded for reinstatement of the compensatory damages, and for additur, because the trial court improperly allowed consideration of restitution by a third party. The court overruled Bader v. Cerri, 96 Nev. 352 (1980), to the extent it would allow admission of such evidence in mitigation of non-consequential damages. The court awarded postjudgment interest.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part, because punitive damages were warranted; reversed in part and remanded for reinstatement of compensatory damages, because another defendant's payments in a separate settlement could not be used as an equitable offset of respondent's damages, and for additur, to the value of the securities converted.

 

581 of 673 DOCUMENTS


Cramer v. Peavy


No. 33128


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 575; 3 P.3d 665; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 78; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 68



July 14, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment entered on a jury verdict and an order of the district court denying a motion for a new trial in a negligence action. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Stephen L. Huffaker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxi driver appealed from a judgment of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), entered on a jury verdict, denying appellant's motion for a mistrial in a negligence action against respondents.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, injured when the taxi he was driving collided with a taxi driven by respondent driver, sued. When the jury found for respondents, appellant, arguing denial of a mistrial was error, appealed. The court affirmed. Nev. Rev. Stat. § 616C.215(10) provided that in any trial of an action by an injured employee against a person other than his employer, the jury had to receive proof of the amount of all payments made by the insurer. Therefore, when the jury was informed that appellant received State Industrial Insurance System (SIIS) benefits, granting a mistrial was not required. Although respondents' references to SIIS payments were improper, they were not so egregious or numerous as to confuse the jury or affect its verdict. Appellant's two additional accidents created a factual question for the jury as to which of appellant's permanent injuries, if any, were caused by the accident in question. Since the jury received ample evidence to support a finding that appellant's permanent injuries were either non-existent or unrelated to his accident, and it was properly instructed on SIIS payments, its verdict was not clearly erroneous.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. The district court did not abuse its discretion in declining to grant a mistrial even though respondent improperly made references concerning state insurance fund payments to appellant. The court found that the references were neither so egregious nor so numerous as to confuse the jury or affect the verdict it reached.

Gaines v. State


No. 33681


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 359; 998 P.2d 166; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 37; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 39



March 13, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 2, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 5618.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a guilty plea of two counts of burglary and one count of forgery. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph S. Pavlikowski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of conviction in the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), pursuant to a guilty plea on two counts of burglary and one count of forgery.


OVERVIEW: The trial court was prohibited from granting the defendant credit for time served in custody on various sentences by a statute and a plea agreement. With respect to Nev. Rev. Stat. § 176.0913, requiring DNA genetic marker testing for certain offenders, the court rejected claimed inapplicability and several constitutional challenges. The statute did not violate: (1) the search warrant requirement because the state's interest in solving crimes outweighed both a convict's diminished expectation of privacy and the minimally intrusive nature of the test; (2) equal protection standards, because of a rational basis for requiring such a test, apprehension of repeat offenders; (3) due process standards because the test was routine and did not concern a fundamental right; (4) cruel and unusual punishment standards because the test was not barbarous and did not involve wanton physical punishment; and (5) overbreadth standards because the statute plainly limited the purpose of testing.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. Trial court did not err in refusing to grant defendant credit for time served in custody on various sentences; court rejected defendant's warrantless search, equal protection, due process, cruel and unusual punishment, and overbreadth constitutional challenges to statute requiring DNA genetic marker testing for certain offenders.

Writ of habeas corpus denied, Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Williams v. Schomig, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 26379 (D. Nev., Mar. 18, 2008)
Post-conviction relief denied at Byford v. State, 126 Nev. 697, 367 P.3d 754, 2010 Nev. LEXIS 1380 (2010)

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of conviction pursuant to a jury verdict of one count of first-degree murder with the use of a deadly weapon and a sentence of death. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Joseph T. Bonaventure, Judge.
Murray v. State, 113 Nev. 11, 930 P.2d 121, 1997 Nev. LEXIS 17 (1997)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his first-degree murder conviction and death sentence from Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada) after his retrial.


OVERVIEW: After his retrial, appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. He appealed on a number of grounds. The appellate court concluded that admitting appellant's testimony from the previous trial was not error because his constitutional rights were not violated and the admission was consistent with the rules of evidence. Although admitting evidence of appellant's prior bad acts was error, reversal was not warranted because the results would have been the same had the evidence not been admitted. The prosecutor's comments related to appellant's prior testimony was harmless error given the overwhelming evidence against him. Although the jury instructions regarding appellant's mens rea for first-degree murder were problematic, the evidence was sufficient for the jury to convict appellant. OUTCOME: Conviction and death sentence were affirmed because trial court did not err in admitting evidence, prosecutor's comments were harmless error, and evidence was sufficient for jury to find necessary mens rea for first-degree murder even though the instructions given were problematic.

Sheriff, Washoe County v. Marcus


No. 32958


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 188; 995 P.2d 1016; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 19; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 19



February 23, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] This is an appeal from an order of the district court granting respondent's pretrial petition for a writ of habeas corpus and dismissing the complaint against respondent. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Deborah A. Agosti, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed an order of the Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada) granting respondent a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged with misdemeanor driving under the influence. After the lower court denied the prosecution a requested continuance, it dismissed the charges without prejudice. Petitioner State then refiled charges against the respondent. Respondent sought a writ of habeas corpus. The lower court granted the writ, and petitioner appealed. The court reversed because Nev. Rev. Stat. § 174.085(5) did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Constitution. Misdemeanor defendants were not a suspect class. Nev. Rev. Stat. § 174.085(5) had a rational basis to the legitimate government interest of giving prosecutors a qualified right to dismiss and refile charges so that evidence could be more fully developed before trial and the grand jury process could be avoided before reinstatement of charges.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's order because the statute giving the prosecutor power to dismiss respondent's misdemeanor charge without prejudice prior to trial did not violate the Equal Protection Clause.

Desimone v. State


No. 24971


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 195; 996 P.2d 405; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 20; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 20



February 23, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction of one count of possession of a trafficking quantity of a controlled substance. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Mills Lane, Judge. Proceedings on remand from the Supreme Court of the United States.

Original Opinion of October 4, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Nev. LEXIS 143.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction reversed and vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the United States Supreme Court, appellant continued to seek review of his conviction of one count of possession of a trafficking quantity of controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with possession and sale of methamphetamine. The lower court entered a civil judgment against him for failure to pay taxes on the drugs pursuant to Nevada's Tax on Controlled Substances Act (CSA). Appellant was later convicted in the lower court of one count of possession of a trafficking quantity of a controlled substance. Appellant sought review of his conviction on the grounds that it was impermissible successive punishment under the Double Jeopardy Clause of the U.S. Constitution, U.S. Const. amend. V. The court vacated appellant's conviction, but the United States Supreme Court vacated the court's decision and remanded. On remand, the court again vacated appellant's conviction. The Nevada legislature intended the CSA as essentially civil in nature. The tax, however, was the functional equivalent of a criminal prosecution. To say otherwise would be to exalt form over substance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and vacated appellant's conviction because appellant's conviction for violating the drug tax law caused his conviction for possession of drugs to be unconstitutional as a violation of the Double Jeopardy Clause.

Knight v. State


No. 31810


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 140; 993 P.2d 67; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 14; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 14



February 3, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count of trespass and one count of carrying a concealed weapon. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; John S. McGroarty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada), which convicted him pursuant to a jury verdict of trespass and carrying a concealed weapon in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 202.350(1)(b).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a jury of trespass and carrying a concealed weapon in violation of Nev. Rev. Stat. § 202.350(1)(b). Appellant had kicked in the door of another's apartment and was found carrying a steak knife and fork. The court overruled previous case law, and held that the appeal was not rendered moot by the fact that appellant had served his sentence because criminal convictions entail adverse collateral consequences. The prosecutor's allegedly improper comments addressed appellant's factual assertions as counsel, and thus the jury could not have necessarily considered them as being directed at appellant's failure to testify. A jury instruction listing a dangerous knife as a weapon under § 202.350(1)(b) was plain error, but the error was harmless. The attendant circumstances unequivocally demonstrated that appellant had been carrying a common steak knife as a weapon.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed on the grounds that the prosecutor's comments did not warrant reversal and that an erroneous jury instruction was harmless because the attendant circumstances unequivocally demonstrated that appellant carried a steak knife as a weapon. Appellant's sentence completion did not render the appeal moot.

KARADANIS v. BOND


No. 33569


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 163; 993 P.2d 721; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 15; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 16



February 3, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a district court order granting respondents' motion to dismiss. Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County; Steven P. Elliott, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Second Judicial District Court, Washoe County (Nevada), which granted respondents' motion to dismiss appellants' complaint and motion for a preliminary injunction seeking to enjoin the enactment of Reno, Nev., Bill No. 1223/Ordinance No. 1047.


OVERVIEW: A railroad grade separation project was approved by the legislature, which authorized a sales tax increase dependent upon the condition precedent of requiring written financial commitments for half the total cost of the project. Appellants challenged Reno, Nevada, Bill No. 1223/Ordinance No. 1047, which implemented the sales tax. The court held that the ordinance was validly enacted because sufficient written financial commitments supported the conclusion that respondent city had met the condition precedent. Curative legislation which ratified the actions of adopting the ordinance did not violate separation of powers principles contained in Nev. Const. art. III, § 1. The curative legislation did not retroactively impact the integrity of a court decision because the legislature merely agreed that the sales tax ordinance was valid.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed because respondent board did not act arbitrarily in ruling that the sales tax ordinance was valid because respondent city had met the condition precedent in the statute. Legislative validation of the act was valid and constitutional.

Graham v. State


No. 31192


SUPREME COURT OF NEVADA


116 Nev. 23; 992 P.2d 255; 2000 Nev. LEXIS 4; 116 Nev. Adv. Rep. 3



January 26, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by, Motion denied by, As moot Graham v. AG of Nev., 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 104045 (D. Nev., July 26, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of conviction, pursuant to a jury verdict, of one count of first-degree murder. Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County; Mark W. Gibbons and Jeffrey D. Sobel, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed jury verdict finding him guilty of murder in first degree of three-year-old child and life sentence from Eighth Judicial District Court, Clark County (Nevada).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of the murder of the eight-month-old daughter of his live-in companion. On appeal, defendant claimed that the trial court erred in refusing to instruct on or provide verdict forms on second-degree murder. The court affirmed the conviction, finding that an enumerated murder, such as murder by child abuse, did not fall within the category of murder that could be reduced in degree by failure to prove intent or deliberation and premeditation. Because the sole agency of death proved in this case was "child abuse," the offense was, by definition, first-degree murder. The court also concluded that the proofs before the jury were only consistent with a finding of either guilty of child-abuse murder or not guilty, thus the use of the involuntary manslaughter instruction without a conforming second-degree murder instruction was harmless error; in fact, the involuntary manslaughter instruction should not have been given.


OUTCOME: First-degree murder conviction affirmed because instruction on second-degree was not erroneously refused since an enumerated murder like child-abuse murder could not be reduced in degree, and because proofs were only consistent with finding on child-abuse murder, it was harmless error to give involuntary manslaughter instruction without companion second-degree instruction.

Fulkerson v. State


No. 69837


COURT OF APPEALS OF NEVADA
